Salah — Prayer
beginner
6 min
Talking Invalidates Prayer: Ruling and Proof
Establishes that talking invalidates prayer with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on talking in prayer and explain how the hadith proves that it invalidates prayer absolutely.

Core Idea
Any speech in prayer invalidates it — whether intentional or forgetful.

This lesson examines why and how talking invalidates prayer according to the Hanafi school. The author provides a clear ruling supported by the Prophet’s hadith, and explains that the prohibition covers all human speech, regardless of intention or amount. Understanding this ruling is essential for every Muslim who prays.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'takallum' (talking) during prayer; the ruling is that it invalidates (yufsid) the prayer.
2
Analyse the hadith proof
The hadith from Muslim states that prayer is not suitable for human speech; the author uses this to derive an absolute prohibition.
'لا يصلح فيها شيء من كلام الناس'
3
Understand the scope: absolute vs. conditional
The author emphasizes that the ruling covers intent, forgetfulness, error, little, and much.
Includes 'العمد والنسيان والخطأ والقليل والكثير'
4
Distinguish between takallum and kalam
The author uses 'takallum' instead of 'kalam' to include even a single word, broadening the scope of invalidating speech.
'التكلم هو النطق' – utterance

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها

يفسد الصلاة التكلم والدعاء بما يشبه كلامنا والأنين والتأوه وارتفاع بكائه من

باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها
لما كان سبق الحدث عارضاً سماوياً والمفسدات عارضاً كسبياً قدم ذاك وآخر هذا، والفساد والبطلان في العبادات سواء .
قوله : (يفسد الصلاة (التكلم الحديث مسلم إن صلاتنا هذه لا يصلح فيها شيء من كلام الناس إنما هو التسبيح والتكبير وقراءة القرآن) . وفي رواية البيهقي «إنما هي» وما لا يصلح فيها مباشرته يفسدها مطلقاً كالأكل والشرب والمكروه غير صالح من وجه دون وجه انتفاء والنص يقتضي الصلاح مطلقاً، أطلقه فشمل العمد والنسيان والخطأ والقليل والكثير لا صلاح صلاته أو لا، عالماً بالتحريم أو لا، ولهذا عبر بالتكلم دون الكلام ليشمل الكلمة الواحدة كما بها في المجمع لأن التكلم هو النطق . يقال تكلم بكلام وتكلم كلاماً. كذا عبر
Chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it.

Talking invalidates prayer, supplication with what resembles our speech, groaning, moaning, and raising of his crying from...

Chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it.
Since the previous occurrence of an event is a celestial contingency and the invalidators are a acquired contingency, that was placed first and this placed last. And corruption and nullification in acts of worship are the same.
His statement: (Talking invalidates prayer) The hadith of Muslim: 'This prayer of ours is not suitable for any human speech; it is only glorification, magnification, and recitation of the Quran.' And in the narration of al-Bayhaqi: 'It is only' and what is not suitable to be done in it invalidates it absolutely, like eating and drinking. And the disliked is not suitable from one aspect while lacking from another aspect. The text necessitates absolute suitability. He made it absolute, so it includes intent, forgetfulness, error, little and much, whether his prayer is valid or not, whether he knows the prohibition or not. For this reason he expressed with 'talking' instead of 'speech' to include the single word as it is in al-Majma', because talking is utterance. One says 'he talked with speech' and 'he talked speech'. Thus he expressed.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked within it. The author first explains that the invalidators are discussed after the causes of necessity because the latter are divine occurrences while the former are human actions. In acts of worship, the terms 'fasad' (corruption) and 'butlan' (nullification) are synonymous—both indicate that the act is no longer valid due to missing an essential element. The main ruling stated is that talking invalidates prayer. The proof provided is the authentic hadith from Muslim: 'This prayer of ours is not suitable for any human speech; it is only glorification, magnification, and recitation of the Quran.' This hadith expressly forbids human speech in prayer, so any such speech invalidates it. The author notes that this applies absolutely—whether done intentionally, forgetfully, or in error; whether little or much; and whether one knows the prohibition or not. He distinguishes between 'takallum' (talking) and 'kalam' (speech), using 'takallum' to encompass even a single word, as mentioned in al-Majma'. The definition is that 'takallum' means utterance, covering both meaningful and meaningless vocalizations.

Key Terms
التكلم (al-takallum): Utterance of any word(s) intentionally or forgetfully, purposeful or not, that resembles human speech.
الكلام (al-kalam): In this context, 'speech' (kalam) is defined as composite sounds that convey a meaning, consisting of at least two letters or a meaningful single letter like 'qif'.
الفساد والبطلان (al-fasad wal-butlan): Both terms are synonymous in acts of worship: the act loses its validity due to missing an essential element (rukn) or condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, what is the ruling on talking in prayer if a person does it forgetfully?
A) It invalidates the prayer.
B) It does not invalidate the prayer but requires sajdah al-sahw.
C) It does not invalidate the prayer and no compensation is needed.
D) It invalidates only if it is two or more words.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that any human speech during prayer invalidates it, based on the absolute prohibition in the hadith.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Position on Talking in Prayer: The Hadith of Dhil-Yadain Rebutted
Inter-madhhab dispute on speech in prayer with intra-Hanafi details

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that all speech invalidates prayer, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the hadith of Dhil-Yadain.

Core Idea
Any speech in prayer, including forgetful, invalidates—the hadith of Dhil-Yadain is abrogated or inapplicable.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq tackles a major inter-madhhab dispute: does talking forgetfully in prayer invalidate it? The Hanafi school holds that any human speech, whether intentional or forgetful, breaks the prayer. The Shafi'i school counters with the famous hadith of Dhil-Yadain, where the Prophet and companions spoke when the Prophet mistakenly gave salaam early. This lesson will guide you through the Hanafi evidence, the intra-Hanafi nuances on blowing and sleep talking, and the strong rebuttals to the hadith of Dhil-Yadain.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling and its proof
First, identify the general Hanafi position: all speech invalidates prayer. The primary proof is the hadith of Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam where the Prophet said, 'This prayer of ours is not suitable for any human speech.'
This hadith is explicit and general, covering intentional and forgetful speech.
2
Examine the intra-Hanafi disputes
The passage details two intra-Hanafi disagreements: blowing (al-nafkh) that produces letters, and talking in sleep. Note that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that blowing with two audible letters invalidates, while sleep talk may not invalidate per the chosen view of many mashayikh.
These nuances do not change the core ruling but show the school's internal ijtihad.
3
Understand the Shafi'i counter-evidence
The Shafi'i school cites the hadith of Dhil-Yadain (from Sahih Muslim) where the Prophet and companions spoke forgetfully, implying that forgetful speech does not nullify prayer.
This seems to directly contradict the Hanafi position.
4
Analyze the Hanafi responses
The Hanafi responses include: (1) the hadith is abrogated because speech was initially permissible; (2) its narrator Abu Hurayra may not have been present; (3) the phrase 'led us' could mean the companions, not himself, since Dhil-Yadain died at Badr before Abu Hurayra embraced Islam. Also, the stronger hadith of Mu'awiya takes precedence.
These are critical refutations based on usul and history.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, any speech invalidates, so one must repeat the prayer if speech occurs. In the Shafi'i school, only deliberate speech invalidates. The mufta bihi Hanafi position is the strict view, based on the general hadith and the rebuttal of Dhil-Yadain.
Knowing this difference is essential for giving fatwa or comparing schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها

النفخ
في ضياء الحلوم وسواء أسمع غيره أو لا، وإن لم يسمع : نفسه وصحح الحروف فعلى قول الكرخي تفسد، وحكي عن الإمام محمد بن الفضل عدمه والاختلاف فيه نظير الاختلاف فيما إذا قرأ في صلاته ولم يسمع نفسه هل تجوز صلاته، وقد بيناه . كذا في الذخيرة. وفي المحيط : المسموع المهجى مفسد عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف لهما أن الكلام اسم لحروف منظومة مسموعة من مخرج الكلام لأن الإفهام بهذا يقع وأدنى ما يقع به انتظام الحروف حرفان اهـ . وينبغي أن يقال : إن أدناه حرفان أو حرف مفهم كـلع أمراً وكذا «ق» فإن فساد الصلاة بهما ظاهر . وشمل
الكلام في النوم وهو قول كثير من المشايخ وهو المختار واختار فخر الإسلام وغيره أنها لا تفسد، وأما ما رواه الحاكم وصححه إن الله وضع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان وما استكرهوا عليه (1) فهو من باب المقتضى ولا عموم له لأنه ضروري فوجب تقديره على وجه يصح والإجماع منعقد على أن رفع الإثم مراد فلا يراد غيره وإلا لزم تعميمه وهو في غير محل الضرورة. ولقائل أن يقول : إن حديث ذي اليدين الثابت في صحيح مسلم فإنه تكلم في الصلاة حين سلم النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم على رأس الركعتين ساهياً وتكلم بعض الصحابة والنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فكان حجة للجمهور بأن كلام الناسي ومن يظن أنه ليس فيها لا يفسدها. فإن أجيب بأن حديث ذي اليدين منسوخ كان في الابتداء حين كان الكلام فيها مباحاً فممنوع لأنه رواية أبي هريرة وهو متأخر الإسلام. وإن أجيب بجواز أن يرويه عن غيره ولم يكن حاضراً فغير صحيح لما في صحيح مسلم عنه «بينا أنا أصلي مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وساق الواقعة وهو صريح في حضوره ولم أر عنه جواباً شافياً. وأراد من التكلم التكلم لغير ضرورة لما سيأتي أنه لو عطس أو تجشأ فحصل منه كلام لا تفسد لتعذر الاحتراز عنه كما في المحيط . ودخل في التكلم المذكور قراءة التوراة والإنجيل والزبور فإنه يفسد كما في الأولوية بل دعواه أن التكلم شامل للكثير الذي دل عليه عبارة المجمع مفهوماً، وللقليل الذي دلت عليه منطوقاً وليس فيه ما يشعر بتقييده بالنحوي أو اللغوي في عبارة المجمع قوله : (وينبغي أن يقال الخ) قد يقال إن ما ذكره من نحوع وق منتظم من حروف تقديراً فهو داخل في تعريف الكلام المذكور تأمل قوله : (ولم أر عنه جواباً شافياً أقول في معراج الدراية : فإن قيل كيف يستقيم هذا فإن راوي حديث ذي اليدين أبو هريرة وهو أسلم بعد فتح خيبر وقد قال أبو هريرة : صلى بنا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وتحريم الكلام كان ثابتاً حين قدم ابن مسعود من الحبشة وذلك في أول الهجرة قلنا : معنى قوله (صلى بنا» أي صلى بأصحابنا ولا وجه للحديث إلا هذا لأن ذا اليدين قتل ببدر واسمه مشهور شهد بدراً وذلك قبل فتح خيبر بزمان طويل . كذا في المبسوط . وانظر ما ذكره الشارح الزيعلي يظهر لك الجواب على أن ما ذكره المؤلف من الرواية قد ذكره في الفتح وغيره من حديث آخر غير حديث ذي اليدين وعبارة الفتح قوله : ولنا قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم إن صلاتنا الخ رواه مسلم من Book of Prayer: Chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it.

Blowing: In Diya al-Huloom: 'Whether he makes others hear or not, and if he does not hear himself but corrects the letters, then according to al-Karkhi it invalidates. It is reported from Imam Muhammad ibn al-Fadl that it does not. The difference is similar to the difference regarding if he recites in his prayer and does not hear himself whether his prayer is valid, and we have explained it.' This is in al-Dhakhira. And in al-Muhit: 'What is heard and pronounced invalidates according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf (apparently a mistake: should be contrary to Abu Hanifa?) because for them speech is the name for arranged letters heard from the vocal outlet, because understanding occurs through this, and the least by which arrangement occurs is two letters.' It should be said: The least is two letters or a meaningful letter like 'qif' as a command, and similarly 'q' for which the invalidation of prayer is apparent. And it includes
talking in sleep, which is the view of many shaykhs and it is the chosen view, but Fakhr al-Islam and others chose that it does not invalidate. As for what al-Hakim narrated and authenticated: 'Indeed Allah has removed from my nation error, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced into' (1), this is from the category of implication and has no generality because it is necessary. So it must be interpreted in a valid manner, and consensus has been established that removal of sin is intended, not other meanings; otherwise it would necessitate generalization and that is not in a place of necessity. And one may say: The hadith of Dhil-Yadain, established in Sahih Muslim, that he (the Prophet) spoke in prayer when the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave salam after two rak'ahs forgetfully, and some Companions spoke, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) [also spoke?], so it is evidence for the majority that the speech of a forgetful person or one who thinks he is not in prayer does not invalidate it. If it is answered that the hadith of Dhil-Yadain is abrogated, as it was in the beginning when speech was permissible in prayer, it is denied because it is narrated by Abu Hurayra who was late in embracing Islam. And if it is answered that it is permissible that he narrated it from someone else and was not present, it is unsound because in Sahih Muslim from him: 'While I was praying with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him)...' and he mentions the story, which is explicit about his presence. And I have not seen a satisfactory answer from him. And he intended by 'talking' unnecessary talking, because it will come later that if one sneezes or belches and speech occurs from it, it does not invalidate due to the impossibility of avoiding it, as in al-Muhit. And included in the mentioned talking is reciting the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms, for it invalidates, as a matter of priority. Rather his claim that 'talking' includes the much that the expression of al-Majma' indicates conceptually, and the little that it indicates explicitly, and there is nothing in it to suggest restriction to grammatical or linguistic definition in the expression of al-Majma'. His statement: 'It should be said...' It may be said that what he mentioned like 'qif' and 'q' are composed of letters in estimation, so they are included in the aforementioned definition of speech. Consider. His statement: 'And I have not seen a satisfactory answer from him.' I say in Mi'raj al-Diraya: If it is said: How can this be sound? The narrator of the hadith of Dhil-Yadain is Abu Hurayra, and he embraced Islam after Khaybar, and Abu Hurayra said: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) led us in prayer...' and the prohibition of speech was established when Ibn Mas'ud came from Abyssinia, which was at the beginning of the Hijra. We say: The meaning of his statement 'led us in prayer' is 'led our companions in prayer', and there is no interpretation for the hadith except this, because Dhul-Yadain was killed at Badr and his name is famous; he witnessed Badr, and that was a long time before Khaybar. This is in al-Mabsut. And look at what al-Sharih al-Zayla'i mentioned; the answer will become clear to you. And the narration that the author mentioned has been mentioned in al-Fath and others from another hadith other than the hadith of Dhil-Yadain. And the wording of al-Fath: 'And for us is his statement (peace be upon him): Indeed our prayer...' narrated by Muslim from the hadith of Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam al-Sulami who said: 'While I was praying with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), suddenly...'
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on what invalidates prayer, focusing on specific cases and points of dispute. The first issue is blowing (al-nafkh) during prayer. According to al-Karkhi, if one blows and produces audible letters, it invalidates prayer; Imam Muhammad ibn al-Fadl holds it does not. The dispute is analogous to whether recitation without hearing oneself is valid. In al-Muhit, audible, articulated speech invalidates according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), contrary to Abu Hanifa? Actually the text says 'khilafan li-Abi Yusuf' but likely should be 'li-Abi Hanifa' as a mistake. The minimum for speech is two letters or a meaningful single letter like 'qif' (stop) or even 'q' (as abbreviation). Talking in sleep: many mashayikh say it invalidates, but Fakhr al-Islam and others choose it does not, citing the hadith that Allah removes error, forgetfulness, and coercion. However, this hadith is not general because it is a necessary concession; consensus holds that only removal of sin is intended. The main inter-school dispute comes with the hadith of Dhil-Yadain. In it, when the Prophet accidentally gave salam after two rak'ahs, a companion (Dhil-Yadain) spoke to inform him, and the Prophet did not reject his speech. The majority (including Shafi'i) use this to argue that speech out of forgetfulness does not invalidate. The Hanafi response is that this hadith is abrogated, or that Abu Hurayra (the narrator) was not present, or that the wordy interpretation shows that 'prayed with us' means with the group, not Abu Hurayra himself, since Dhil-Yadain died at Badr. The passage concludes by noting that the correct proof is the hadith of Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam, which clearly prohibits speech.

Key Terms
النفخ (al-nafkh): Blowing air with the mouth, potentially producing audible letters. If it produces intelligible speech, it invalidates prayer according to some Hanafi scholars.
الكلام في النوم (al-kalam fi al-nawm): Uttering words while asleep during prayer. There is disagreement among Hanafi scholars whether it invalidates prayer.
حديث ذي اليدين (hadith Dhi al-Yadayn): The hadith about the companion 'the one with two hands' who spoke to the Prophet when he gave salam early. Used by Shafi'i as evidence that forgetful speech does not invalidate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi proof that all speech, including forgetful, invalidates prayer?
A) The hadith of Dhil-Yadain
B) The hadith of Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam
C) The hadith of 'Allah has removed error and forgetfulness'
D) The consensus of the Sahaba

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that any human speech in prayer, even if forgetful, invalidates it, and the hadith of Dhil-Yadain is rebutted on historical and usul grounds.

Salah — Prayer

Supplication That Resembles Human Speech in Prayer
Explaining the ruling on supplication that resembles human speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion for distinguishing between supplication that invalidates prayer and supplication that does not.

Core Idea
Supplication invalidates prayer if it asks for something possible to request from humans.

In this passage, the commentator of Kanz al-Daqā’iq explains when du’ā (supplication) during prayer becomes invalidating speech. The key distinction is whether the content could be requested from another human being. This lesson will guide you through that criterion and its implications, including a cross-madhhab note against al-Shāfi‘ī.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on supplication in prayer
The base text (matn) states that supplication that resembles human speech is separated from the general rule of speech because Imam al-Shāfi‘ī does not invalidate prayer by du’ā. The Hanafi position is that such du’ā invalidates if it can be asked from creatures.
The phrase 'afradahu' indicates the author deliberately isolates this case.
2
Define 'what resembles our speech'
The commentary gives a clear definition: supplication that is possible to ask from servants (e.g., 'O Allah, feed me' or 'pay my debt') resembles human speech. Supplication for what is impossible to ask from humans (e.g., health, forgiveness) does not.
Al-Muhīt confirms this applies whether the du’ā is for oneself or for another.
3
Apply the criterion to examples
Apply the rule: du’ā from the Qur’ān, transmitted du’ās, or requests impossible from humans (e.g., 'O Allah, forgive me') do not invalidate. Requests possible from humans but not in the transmitted texts (e.g., 'O Allah, marry me to so-and-so') do invalidate.
An exception noted: 'O Allah, forgive my uncle' is transmitted as invalidating by agreement, despite being impossible from humans – this is a problematic case discussed later.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Al-Shāfi‘ī holds that du’ā never invalidates prayer, even if it resembles human speech. The Hanafi response is to maintain the distinction based on the nature of the request.
This difference affects practical fatwa in multi-madhhab contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها وجع أو مصيبة لا من ذكر جنة أو نار والتنحنح بلا عذر وجواب عاطس بيرحمك الله المجتبي. وقال في الأصل : لم يجزه. وفي جامع الكرخي فس فسدت . وعن أبي يوسف إن أشبه التسبيح جاز . قوله : (والدعاء بما يشبه كلامنا أفرده وإن دخل في التكلم لأن الشافعي لا يفسدها بالدعاء. وينبغي أن يتعلق قوله بما يشبه كلامنا بالتكلم والدعاء. وقد قدمنا بأن الدعاء بما يشبه كلامنا هو ما أمكن سؤاله من العباد كاللهم اطعمني أو اقض ديني وارزقني فلانة على الصحيح، وما استحال طلبه من العباد فليس من كلامنا مثل العافية والمغفرة والرزق، سواء كان لنفسه أو لغيره ولو لأخيه على الصحيح كما في المحيط . وفي الظهيرية : ولو قال أل ثم قال الحمد لله أو لم يقل لا تفسد صلاته. وقال المرغيناني : إن أنصاف الكلمة مثل كل الكلمة تفسد صلاته، ثم ذكر ضابطاً للدعاء بما يشبه كلامنا فقال : الحاصل أنه إذا دعا بما جاء في الصلاة أو في القرآن أو في المأثور لا تفسد صلاته، وإن لم يكن في القرآن أو في المأثور ولا يستحيل سؤاله تفسد، وإن كان يستحيل سؤاله لا تفسد .اهـ. ويشكل عليه اللهم اغفر لعمي أو خالي فإنه نقل أنها تفسد اتفاقاً كما قدمناه قوله : (والأنين والتاوه وارتفاع بكائه من وجع أو مصيبة لا من ذكر جنة أو نار) أي عطس رجل من القوم إلى آخر ما ذكره. وأظن أن المؤلف اشتبه عليه هذا الحديث بحديث ذي اليدين Book of Prayer, Chapter of What Invalidates Prayer and What is Disliked Therein. Pain or calamity, not from mention of Paradise or Fire, and clearing the throat without excuse, and responding to a sneezer with 'May Allah have mercy on you' from al-Mujtaba. He said in al-Asl: It does not permit him. In Jami' al-Karkhi: it becomes invalid. From Abu Yusuf: if it resembles tasbih, it is permissible. His statement: (And supplication that resembles our speech he separates, even if it enters into speaking, because al-Shafi'i does not invalidate it by supplication. And it is necessary that his statement 'resembles our speech' be attached to speaking and supplication. And we have already that supplication that resembles our speech is what is possible to ask from the servants, like 'O Allah, feed me' or 'pay my debt' and 'provide me with such-and-such' according to the correct view, and what is impossible to ask from the servants is not from our speech, like health, forgiveness, and provision, whether for oneself or others, even for his brother according to the correct view as in al-Muhit. And in al-Zahiriyya: if he said 'al' then said 'al-hamdulillah' or did not say, his prayer does not invalidate. And al-Marghinani said: half of a word is like the whole word, it invalidates his prayer. Then he mentioned a criterion for supplication resembling our speech and said: The conclusion is that if he supplicates with what came in the prayer or in the Quran or in the transmitted, his prayer does not invalidate; if it is not in the Quran or transmitted and it is not impossible to ask, it invalidates; and if it is impossible to ask, it does not invalidate. End. And it is problematic for him: 'O Allah forgive my paternal uncle or my maternal uncle' for it is transmitted that it invalidates by agreement, as we have already. His statement: (And moaning and sighing and raising his crying from pain or calamity, not from mention of Paradise or Fire) meaning a man sneezed from the people... to the end of what he mentioned. And I think the author confused this hadith with the hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn; so let him review. His statement: 'And it enters into the mentioned speaking the reading of the Torah etc.' He said in al-Nahr: I say: it is necessary to interpret what is in al-Mujtaba as the substituted from it if it is not remembrance or glorification, and it has preceded that other than the substituted is prohibited for the junub to read. His statement: (And it is necessary that it be attached etc.) He said in al-Nahr: The apparent from what is in al-Sharh and al-Ayni followed it, that it is a restriction on supplication only, which is apparent because supplication includes what resembles our speech and what does not, unlike speaking, for it invalidates even if it does not resemble our speech, like meaningless utterance, and there is no doubt that it being a restriction in it excludes it, so ponder. And al-Ghunaymi criticized him with what he presented that the meaning of 'speaking' is pronouncing letters, whether called speech or not, as if he forgot that and also forgot his presentation to his insightful brother, where he said: This, meaning the author's expression with 'takallum' (speaking) is better than the expression of al-Majma' with 'kalam' (speech) where he said in objection to that: and there is consideration, since it is based on the meaning being grammatical and it is not definite that linguistic meaning is intended, rather it is apparent. End of his objection. So you see he considered that the intended meaning is linguistic speech, and then his claim that meaningless utterance does not resemble people's speech is prevented; rather it resembles their speech linguistically because it is a sound containing letters. And his statement 'there is no doubt that it being a restriction in it excludes it' you have known from what preceded that it being a restriction in it includes it. Thus in the marginalia of Sharh Miskin. His statement: (And al-Marghinani said etc.) I say: He said in al-Tajnis: If he stops at half a word then resumes, his prayer does not invalidate even if the meaning of the half is ugly due to necessity. And in Zallat al-Qari from Fath al-Qadir from al-Khaniyya: If he intended to read a word and half a word flowed on his tongue, then he returns and reads the first or bows and does not complete it, if it was half a word that if he completed it would not invalidate his prayer, it does not invalidate; and if it would invalidate if he completed it, it invalidates. End. And half has the ruling of the whole, and that is correct. End. The author's statement: 'and raising his crying'. He said in al-Nahr: In al-Sihah, 'al-buka' is lengthened and shortened; if you lengthen you mean the sound that accompanies crying, and if you shorten you mean tears and their exit. [PAGE_006] ... and with it it invalidates. As for moaning, it is that he says 'ah' as in al-Kafi, and sighing (ta'awwuh) is that he says 'oh', and it is said 'awwaha al-rajul' (he sighed) and 'ta'awwaha' if he said 'oh'. And it is said in al-Maghrib: it is a word of pain. And a man is 'awwah', much sighing. And al-Halabi mentioned in Sharh al-Munya that it has thirteen languages: the hamza is fatha in all of them, then it may be lengthened or not, with tashdid of the waw with fatha and sukun of the ha, those are two languages; without lengthening with tashdid of the waw with kasra and sukun of the ha and its kasra, those are another two; with sukun of the waw and kasra of the ha, that is a fifth; tashdid of the waw with fatha and kasra without ha, those are a sixth and seventh; and 'aw' like the conjunctive 'aw', that is an eighth, and it is lengthened but followed by a silent ha and a kasra without waw, those are a ninth and tenth; and the eleventh and twelfth: 'awyah' with lengthening of the hamza and without, and fatha of the waw with tashdid followed by ya, then alif, then silent ha; and the thirteenth: 'aww' with lengthening and damm of the first waw and sukun of the second followed by silent ha. Then, naming 'ah' as anin and 'oh' as ta'awwuh is terminological. End. Meaning not linguistic, because among the languages of ta'awwuh is 'ah' which is the tenth. As for raising the crying, it is that letters occur from it. And his statement 'from pain or calamity' is a restriction for the three; and his statement 'not from mention of Paradise or Fire' also returns to all. So the conclusion: if it is from mention of Paradise or Fire, it indicates increase in humility, and if he explicitly states them saying 'O Allah, I ask You for Paradise and seek refuge with You from the Fire', his prayer does not invalidate. And if it is from pain or calamity, it indicates manifesting them, so it is as if he said 'I am afflicted', and indication operates like explicit statement when there is no explicit statement to the contrary. And this all according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And from Abu Yusuf that his saying 'ah' does not invalidate in both cases, and 'oh' invalidates; and it is said that his principle is that if the word contains two letters and they are extra or one of them, it does not invalidate, and if both are original, it invalidates. And the extra letters are collected in our saying 'aman wa tashil' (safety and facilitation). And by 'extra' we mean that if a letter were added to the word, it would be among these letters, not that these letters are extra wherever they occur. He said in al-Hidaya: The statement of Abu Yusuf is not strong because people's speech in their understanding... His statement: (So it is that he says 'ah'). He said in al-Nahr: Moaning is the sound of the one in pain, thus in al-Inaya. Al-Ayni specified it as resulting from his saying 'ah'; and it is said that it is his saying 'ah' with shortening of the hamza with fatha as in Sharh al-Munya of Sheikh Ibrahim al-Halabi, and likewise in al-Sharnablaliyya from Taj al-Shari'a, and he added that it is the moaning of the non-Arabs, and it is on the pattern of 'da''. This is the understanding from the speech of al-Inaya where he made it two letters during the discussion of the matn. His statement: (Thirteen). I say: the copy of al-Ramli was 'three thirteen' (thalatha 'ashar), so he objected that the correct is 'thalath 'ashrata'. His statement: (Then naming 'ah' as anin and 'oh' as ta'awwuh is terminological). He said in al-Nahr: You are aware that this only applies based on what has passed that it is the word 'ah'; but if it is the sound of the one in pain, then the difference... I say: likewise the difference based on what has passed that it is the word 'ah', because what is here is lengthened and what passed was shortened as you know from what we transmitted from Sharh al-Munya and al-Sharnablaliyya. His statement: (And the extra letters are collected etc.). Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses what invalidates prayer (salat) by verbal actions. The author first presents the matn (base text) listing: moaning (anīn), sighing (ta'awwuh), crying (irtifā' bukā') due to pain or calamity (as opposed to due to fear of Paradise or Hell), clearing the throat without excuse (tanahhun bida 'udhr), and responding to a sneezer with 'yarhamukallah'. The initial commentary notes a ikhtilāf: al-Mujtaba reports that these do not invalidate, while al-Asl says they do. In Jami' al-Karkhi the prayer becomes invalid. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the sound resembles tasbīh (glorification), it is permissible. The commentary then moves to the main discussion on du'ā (supplication) that resembles human speech (mā yushbihu kalāmanā). The matn states that such supplication is separated from speech (afradahu) even if it enters into the category of speaking (takallum), because al-Shāfi'ī does not invalidate prayer by du'ā. The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'resembles our speech' applies to both speech and supplication. He then defines the criterion: supplication that can be requested from human beings (like asking for food or debt) invalidates if spoken, while what cannot be requested from humans (like health, forgiveness, or provision from Allah alone) does not resemble human speech and thus does not invalidate. This applies whether one prays for himself or others, even his brother, as per al-Muhīt. The commentary then discusses various opinions: al-Zāhira: saying 'al' then 'al-hamdulillah' does not invalidate. Al-Marghinānī states that half a word is like the whole word and invalidates. He gives a criterion: supplication that is found in the prayer, the Quran, or transmitted traditions does not invalidate; if not found and not impossible to ask, it invalidates; if impossible to ask, it does not. An objection is raised: saying 'O Allah forgive my uncle' is transmitted to invalidate by agreement, contradicting the criterion. The commentary then returns to the matn's clause on moaning, sighing, and crying. It provides lexical definitions: anīn is saying 'ah' (with a short alif), ta'awwuh is saying 'oh' (or 'awh'), and there are thirteen linguistic forms for these interjections. The meaning of 'raising crying' involves producing letters (vocalization). The key distinction: if the crying or moaning arises from fear of Paradise or Hell, it indicates increased humility (khushū') and does not invalidate; if from pain or calamity, it indicates complaint and invalidates according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn). Abu Yusuf has a separate view: 'ah' does not invalidate in either case, while 'oh' does. His underlying principle is that words consisting of only extra letters (hurūf al-zawā'id) do not invalidate. The extra letters are summarized by the phrase 'amān wa tashīl' (alif, mīm, alif, nūn, etc.). However, al-Hidāya criticizes Abu Yusuf's view as weak because people's speech, even these interjections, is considered speech in common understanding. The commentary concludes that the terminology of calling 'ah' as anīn and 'oh' as ta'awwuh is conventional, not purely linguistic. The overall lesson is that the validity of prayer depends on the nature of the utterance: whether it is deliberate speech seeking to communicate, which invalidates, or involuntary expressions of pain or spiritual emotion, which generally do not.

Key Terms
الدعاء بما يشبه كلامنا (al-du'ā' bimā yushbihu kalāmanā): Supplication that resembles human speech, i.e., something that could be requested from human beings.
التكلم (al-takallum): Speaking; in prayer, deliberate speech invalidates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi criterion, which of the following supplications would INVALIDATE the prayer if spoken during salah?
A) 'O Allah, forgive me and have mercy on me.'
B) 'O Allah, provide for my family.'
C) 'O Allah, marry me to so-and-so.'
D) 'O Allah, guide me to the straight path.'

Key Takeaway
The validity of supplication in prayer depends on content: if you could ask a human for the same thing, it invalidates; if not, it remains valid.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
10 min
Moaning, Sighing, and Crying in Prayer: When Do They Invalidate?
Explaining the rulings on moaning, sighing, and crying in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between moaning, sighing, and crying that invalidate prayer and those that do not, based on the cause (pain/calamity vs. fear of Hereafter) and the nature of the utterance.

Core Idea
Moaning and crying invalidate prayer when due to pain or calamity, but not when due to fear of Paradise or Fire.

This lesson unpacks a detailed fiqh discussion on whether involuntary sounds like 'ah', 'oh', and crying during prayer break the prayer. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the trigger: if the sound arises from physical pain or worldly calamity, it is considered a complaint and invalidates; if from consciousness of the Hereafter, it is a sign of devotion and does not invalidate. There is also an intra-Hanafi difference regarding the specific words 'ah' and 'oh'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three actions: moaning, sighing, crying
The matn lists anīn (moaning), ta'awwuh (sighing), and irtifā' al-bukā' (raising of crying) as actions that may invalidate prayer.
Crying is specifically 'raising' i.e., producing audible letters.
2
Apply the key distinction: cause of the sound
If the moaning, sighing, or crying is due to pain or a worldly calamity, it invalidates. If due to reflection on Paradise or Hell, it indicates humility and does not invalidate.
This is based on the principle that indicating complaint or informing others of one's state is a form of speech.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on 'ah' and 'oh'
Abu Yusuf distinguishes: the word 'ah' contains extra letters (alif, ha) so it is not considered true speech and does not invalidate; 'oh' has original letters and does invalidate. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that both invalidate because they are understood as speech.
Al-Hidaya criticizes Abu Yusuf's view, saying that people's speech includes such interjections.
4
Understand the linguistic technicalities
The commentary provides extensive lugha (philology) on the various pronunciations of 'ah' and 'oh', but the fiqh ruling depends on whether the utterance constitutes speech (kalām) in the common understanding.
The terminology of anīn vs. ta'awwuh is conventional, not strictly linguistic.

التسبيح جاز . قوله : (والدعاء بما يشبه كلامنا أفرده وإن دخل في التكلم لأن الشافعي لا يفسدها بالدعاء. وينبغي أن يتعلق قوله بما يشبه كلامنا بالتكلم والدعاء. وقد قدمنا بأن الدعاء بما يشبه كلامنا هو ما أمكن سؤاله من العباد كاللهم اطعمني أو اقض ديني وارزقني فلانة على الصحيح، وما استحال طلبه من العباد فليس من كلامنا مثل العافية والمغفرة والرزق، سواء كان لنفسه أو لغيره ولو لأخيه على الصحيح كما في Book of Prayer, Chapter of What Invalidates Prayer and What is Disliked Therein. Pain or calamity, not from mention of Paradise or Fire, and clearing the throat without excuse, and responding to a sneezer with 'May Allah have mercy on you' from al-Mujtaba. He said in al-Asl: It does not permit him. In Jami' al-Karkhi: it becomes invalid. From Abu Yusuf: if it resembles tasbih, it is permissible. His statement: (And supplication that resembles our speech he separates, even if it enters into speaking, because al-Shafi'i does not invalidate it by supplication. And it is necessary that his statement 'resembles our speech' be attached to speaking and supplication. And we have already that supplication that resembles our speech is what is possible to ask from the servants, like 'O Allah, feed me' or 'pay my debt' and 'provide me with such-and-such' according to the correct view, and what is impossible to ask from the servants is not from our speech, like health, forgiveness, and provision, whether for oneself or others, even for his brother according to the correct view as in al-Muhit. And in al-Zahiriyya: if he said 'al' then said 'al-hamdulillah' or did not say, his prayer does not invalidate. And al-Marghinani said: half of a word is like the whole word, it invalidates his prayer. Then he mentioned a criterion for supplication resembling our speech and said: The conclusion is that if he supplicates with what came in the prayer or in the Quran or in the transmitted, his prayer does not invalidate; if it is not in the Quran or transmitted and it is not impossible to ask, it invalidates; and if it is impossible to ask, it does not invalidate. End. And it is problematic for him: 'O Allah forgive my paternal uncle or my maternal uncle' for it is transmitted that it invalidates by agreement, as we have already. His statement: (And moaning and sighing and raising his crying from pain or calamity, not from mention of Paradise or Fire) meaning a man sneezed from the people... to the end of what he mentioned. And I think the author confused this hadith with the hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn; so let him review. His statement: 'And it enters into the mentioned speaking the reading of the Torah etc.' He said in al-Nahr: I say: it is necessary to interpret what is in al-Mujtaba as the substituted from it if it is not remembrance or glorification, and it has preceded that other than the substituted is prohibited for the junub to read. His statement: (And it is necessary that it be attached etc.) He said in al-Nahr: The apparent from what is in al-Sharh and al-Ayni followed it, that it is a restriction on supplication only, which is apparent because supplication includes what resembles our speech and what does not, unlike speaking, for it invalidates even if it does not resemble our speech, like meaningless utterance, and there is no doubt that it being a restriction in it excludes it, so ponder. And al-Ghunaymi criticized him with what he presented that the meaning of 'speaking' is pronouncing letters, whether called speech or not, as if he forgot that and also forgot his presentation to his insightful brother, where he said: This, meaning the author's expression with 'takallum' (speaking) is better than the expression of al-Majma' with 'kalam' (speech) where he said in objection to that: and there is consideration, since it is based on the meaning being grammatical and it is not definite that linguistic meaning is intended, rather it is apparent. End of his objection. So you see he considered that the intended meaning is linguistic speech, and then his claim that meaningless utterance does not resemble people's speech is prevented; rather it resembles their speech linguistically because it is a sound containing letters. And his statement 'there is no doubt that it being a restriction in it excludes it' you have known from what preceded that it being a restriction in it includes it. Thus in the marginalia of Sharh Miskin. His statement: (And al-Marghinani said etc.) I say: He said in al-Tajnis: If he stops at half a word then resumes, his prayer does not invalidate even if the meaning of the half is ugly due to necessity. And in Zallat al-Qari from Fath al-Qadir from al-Khaniyya: If he intended to read a word and half a word flowed on his tongue, then he returns and reads the first or bows and does not complete it, if it was half a word that if he completed it would not invalidate his prayer, it does not invalidate; and if it would invalidate if he completed it, it invalidates. End. And half has the ruling of the whole, and that is correct. End. The author's statement: 'and raising his crying'. He said in al-Nahr: In al-Sihah, 'al-buka' is lengthened and shortened; if you lengthen you mean the sound that accompanies crying, and if you shorten you mean tears and their exit. [PAGE_006] ... and with it it invalidates. As for moaning, it is that he says 'ah' as in al-Kafi, and sighing (ta'awwuh) is that he says 'oh', and it is said 'awwaha al-rajul' (he sighed) and 'ta'awwaha' if he said 'oh'. And it is said in al-Maghrib: it is a word of pain. And a man is 'awwah', much sighing. And al-Halabi mentioned in Sharh al-Munya that it has thirteen languages: the hamza is fatha in all of them, then it may be lengthened or not, with tashdid of the waw with fatha and sukun of the ha, those are two languages; without lengthening with tashdid of the waw with kasra and sukun of the ha and its kasra, those are another two; with sukun of the waw and kasra of the ha, that is a fifth; tashdid of the waw with fatha and kasra without ha, those are a sixth and seventh; and 'aw' like the conjunctive 'aw', that is an eighth, and it is lengthened but followed by a silent ha and a kasra without waw, those are a ninth and tenth; and the eleventh and twelfth: 'awyah' with lengthening of the hamza and without, and fatha of the waw with tashdid followed by ya, then alif, then silent ha; and the thirteenth: 'aww' with lengthening and damm of the first waw and sukun of the second followed by silent ha. Then, naming 'ah' as anin and 'oh' as ta'awwuh is terminological. End. Meaning not linguistic, because among the languages of ta'awwuh is 'ah' which is the tenth. As for raising the crying, it is that letters occur from it. And his statement 'from pain or calamity' is a restriction for the three; and his statement 'not from mention of Paradise or Fire' also returns to all. So the conclusion: if it is from mention of Paradise or Fire, it indicates increase in humility, and if he explicitly states them saying 'O Allah, I ask You for Paradise and seek refuge with You from the Fire', his prayer does not invalidate. And if it is from pain or calamity, it indicates manifesting them, so it is as if he said 'I am afflicted', and indication operates like explicit statement when there is no explicit statement to the contrary. And this all according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And from Abu Yusuf that his saying 'ah' does not invalidate in both cases, and 'oh' invalidates; and it is said that his principle is that if the word contains two letters and they are extra or one of them, it does not invalidate, and if both are original, it invalidates. And the extra letters are collected in our saying 'aman wa tashil' (safety and facilitation). And by 'extra' we mean that if a letter were added to the word, it would be among these letters, not that these letters are extra wherever they occur. He said in al-Hidaya: The statement of Abu Yusuf is not strong because people's speech in their understanding... His statement: (So it is that he says 'ah'). He said in al-Nahr: Moaning is the sound of the one in pain, thus in al-Inaya. Al-Ayni specified it as resulting from his saying 'ah'; and it is said that it is his saying 'ah' with shortening of the hamza with fatha as in Sharh al-Munya of Sheikh Ibrahim al-Halabi, and likewise in al-Sharnablaliyya from Taj al-Shari'a, and he added that it is the moaning of the non-Arabs, and it is on the pattern of 'da''. This is the understanding from the speech of al-Inaya where he made it two letters during the discussion of the matn. His statement: (Thirteen). I say: the copy of al-Ramli was 'three thirteen' (thalatha 'ashar), so he objected that the correct is 'thalath 'ashrata'. His statement: (Then naming 'ah' as anin and 'oh' as ta'awwuh is terminological). He said in al-Nahr: You are aware that this only applies based on what has passed that it is the word 'ah'; but if it is the sound of the one in pain, then the difference... I say: likewise the difference based on what has passed that it is the word 'ah', because what is here is lengthened and what passed was shortened as you know from what we transmitted from Sharh al-Munya and al-Sharnablaliyya. His statement: (And the extra letters are collected etc.). He said in al-Nahr: Sheikh Sha'ban said in his correction of Al-Fiyyat Ibn Mu'ti: that they have been collected in twenty collections and he listed them, but some are criticized, and no one has collected them four times except Ibn Malik in Sharh al-Kafiya where he said:

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses what invalidates prayer (salat) by verbal actions. The author first presents the matn (base text) listing: moaning (anīn), sighing (ta'awwuh), crying (irtifā' bukā') due to pain or calamity (as opposed to due to fear of Paradise or Hell), clearing the throat without excuse (tanahhun bida 'udhr), and responding to a sneezer with 'yarhamukallah'. The initial commentary notes a ikhtilāf: al-Mujtaba reports that these do not invalidate, while al-Asl says they do. In Jami' al-Karkhi the prayer becomes invalid. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the sound resembles tasbīh (glorification), it is permissible. The commentary then moves to the main discussion on du'ā (supplication) that resembles human speech (mā yushbihu kalāmanā). The matn states that such supplication is separated from speech (afradahu) even if it enters into the category of speaking (takallum), because al-Shāfi'ī does not invalidate prayer by du'ā. The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'resembles our speech' applies to both speech and supplication. He then defines the criterion: supplication that can be requested from human beings (like asking for food or debt) invalidates if spoken, while what cannot be requested from humans (like health, forgiveness, or provision from Allah alone) does not resemble human speech and thus does not invalidate. This applies whether one prays for himself or others, even his brother, as per al-Muhīt. The commentary then discusses various opinions: al-Zāhira: saying 'al' then 'al-hamdulillah' does not invalidate. Al-Marghinānī states that half a word is like the whole word and invalidates. He gives a criterion: supplication that is found in the prayer, the Quran, or transmitted traditions does not invalidate; if not found and not impossible to ask, it invalidates; if impossible to ask, it does not. An objection is raised: saying 'O Allah forgive my uncle' is transmitted to invalidate by agreement, contradicting the criterion. The commentary then returns to the matn's clause on moaning, sighing, and crying. It provides lexical definitions: anīn is saying 'ah' (with a short alif), ta'awwuh is saying 'oh' (or 'awh'), and there are thirteen linguistic forms for these interjections. The meaning of 'raising crying' involves producing letters (vocalization). The key distinction: if the crying or moaning arises from fear of Paradise or Hell, it indicates increased humility (khushū') and does not invalidate; if from pain or calamity, it indicates complaint and invalidates according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn). Abu Yusuf has a separate view: 'ah' does not invalidate in either case, while 'oh' does. His underlying principle is that words consisting of only extra letters (hurūf al-zawā'id) do not invalidate. The extra letters are summarized by the phrase 'amān wa tashīl' (alif, mīm, alif, nūn, etc.). However, al-Hidāya criticizes Abu Yusuf's view as weak because people's speech, even these interjections, is considered speech in common understanding. The commentary concludes that the terminology of calling 'ah' as anīn and 'oh' as ta'awwuh is conventional, not purely linguistic. The overall lesson is that the validity of prayer depends on the nature of the utterance: whether it is deliberate speech seeking to communicate, which invalidates, or involuntary expressions of pain or spiritual emotion, which generally do not.

Key Terms
الأنين (al-anīn): Moaning, specifically the utterance 'ah' (أه) as a sound of pain.
التأوه (al-ta'awwuh): Sighing, specifically the utterance 'oh' (أوه) as a sound of pain.
ارتفاع البكاء (irtifā' al-bukā'): Raising the voice in crying, i.e., producing audible letters (not just tears).
حروف الزوائد (ḥurūf al-zawā'id): Extra letters in Arabic morphology: alif, lām, etc. In this context, letters that are not original to a word's root.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person in prayer begins to cry audibly due to intense fear of Allah's punishment. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the prayer?
A) The prayer is invalidated because crying with audible letters is considered speech.
B) The prayer is valid because the crying is due to fear of the Hereafter, which indicates increased devotion.
C) The prayer is invalid only if the crying was accompanied by words.
D) The prayer is valid only if the person tried to suppress the crying.

Key Takeaway
Moaning, sighing, and crying invalidate prayer only when they are due to worldly pain or calamity, not when arising from spiritual reflection on the Hereafter.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
6 min
Ruling on Saying 'Af' (Ta'fif) in Prayer and the Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ruling on ta'fif in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on saying 'Af' in prayer and identify the differing position of Abu Yusuf regarding its invalidation.

Core Idea
Saying 'Af' in prayer invalidates it according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, but Abu Yusuf makes an exception based on letter count.

In prayer, any speech that is not part of the ritual is generally invalidating. But what about a simple expression of annoyance like 'Af'? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on this point, with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad taking a strict view and Abu Yusuf offering a nuanced exception. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the general ruling
The act is saying 'Af' (or 'Kaf', 'Taf') during prayer. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, any such utterance invalidates prayer regardless of intent or emphasis.
2
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that saying 'Af' does not automatically invalidate. His view is based on a letter-count principle: utterances of two letters (or less) do not invalidate; those of four letters do; three letters are disputed but the preferred view is no invalidation.
There are two narrations from Abu Yusuf: one based on emphasis (shaddah), the other on letter count.
3
Analyze the basis of the dispute
The core difference lies in what constitutes invalidating speech. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider 'Af' as meaningful speech outside the prayer, while Abu Yusuf applies a quantitative measure (letter count) to determine whether the utterance is negligible.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: saying 'Af' invalidates prayer absolutely. This is the view taken in the main text and supported by the majority of later scholars.
Fatwa is given on this view due to its strength in transmission (zahir al-riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتأفيف كالأنين كأف وتف . ثم «أف» اسم فعل لأتضجر، وقيل لتضجرت، وسواء أراد به تنقية موضع سجوده أو أراد به التأفيف فإن الصلاة تفسد عندهما مطلقاً. وقال أبو يوسف بعدمه، لكن في المجتبى : الصحيح أن خلافه إنما هو في المخفف وفي المشدد تفسد عندهم ويعارضه ما في الخلاصة أن الأصل عنده أن في الحرفين لا تفسد صلاته، وفي أربعة أحرف تفسد، وفي ثلاثة أحرف اختلف المشايخ فيها والأصح أنها لا تفسد اهـ.
And al-ta'fif is like al-anīn: (saying) 'kaf' and 'taf'. Then 'af' is a verb form expressing annoyance, and it is said meaning 'I am annoyed'. Whether one intends to clean his prostration place or intends al-ta'fif, the prayer is invalidated according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) absolutely. Abu Yusuf said it does not invalidate. However, in al-Mujtaba: the correct view is that his disagreement is only in the light (without shaddah), and with shaddah it is invalidated according to them. And contradicting it is what is in al-Khulasa: his principle is that in two letters the prayer is not invalidated, in four letters it is invalidated, and in three letters the scholars differed and the correct view is that it is not invalidated.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of saying 'Af' in prayer. 'Af' is a word expressing disgust or annoyance. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (al-Sahibayn), saying 'Af' in prayer invalidates the prayer absolutely, regardless of intent. Abu Yusuf holds that it does not invalidate. However, reports differ on the details of Abu Yusuf's view: al-Mujtaba states that his disagreement applies only to the unemphasized form (without shaddah), while the emphasized form (with shaddah) invalidates. Al-Khulasa provides a principle based on letter count: utterances with two letters (even if extra) do not invalidate, but those with four letters do; three letters are disputed, but the correct view is that they do not invalidate. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and al-Sahibayn, as stated in the main text.

Key Terms
تأفيف (ta'fif): The act of saying 'Af' (or 'Kaf', 'Taf') as an expression of annoyance.
أف (af): A word meaning 'I am annoyed' or 'expression of disgust'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position, if a person in prayer says 'Af' to express annoyance at a fly, what is the ruling?
A) Prayer is valid
B) Prayer is invalid
C) Prayer is valid only if he says it lightly
D) Prayer is invalid only if he says it twice

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that saying 'Af' in prayer invalidates it, though Abu Yusuf has a nuanced exception based on letter count.

Ruling on Clearing the Throat in Prayer
Explaining that clearing the throat without excuse invalidates prayer, but with excuse it does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on clearing the throat (tanahhun) in prayer and identify the condition (excuse) that changes the ruling.

Core Idea
Clearing the throat invalidates prayer only if done without a valid excuse.

Clearing the throat during prayer is a common concern. The Hanafi school provides a clear distinction: if done without a legitimate excuse, it invalidates the prayer; if due to necessity, it does not. This lesson clarifies the definition of tanahhun and udhr, and the unanimous ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage discusses 'tanahhun' (clearing throat). The ruling is binary: without excuse it invalidates; with excuse it does not.
This is a direct ruling from the text.
2
Examine the definition of tanahhun
The passage defines tanahhun as saying 'Ahu' with fatha or damma. Understand that it is a deliberate vocalization.
The sound resembles 'Ahu'.
3
Understand the concept of excuse (udhr)
An excuse is a circumstance that befalls the obligated person, necessitating leniency. Examples: illness or need to clear phlegm.
The excuse transforms the ruling from invalidating to permissible.
4
Confirm the unanimous agreement
The text states 'bila khilaf' (without dispute). This ruling is agreed upon within the Hanafi school.
There is no intra-Hanafi disagreement on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتنحنح بلا عذر وهو أن يقول أح بالفتح والضم والعذر وصف يطرأ على المكلف يناسب التسهيل عليه، فإن كان التنحنح لعذر فإنه لا يبطل الصلاة بلا خلاف وإن حصل.
And clearing the throat without excuse: it is to say 'Ahu' with fatha and damma. Excuse is a circumstance that occurs to the obligated person necessitating leniency. If clearing the throat is due to an excuse, it does not invalidate the prayer without dispute, even if it occurs.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling on clearing the throat (tanahhun) during prayer. It defines it as producing the sound 'Ahu' (with fatha or damma on the first letter). The ruling is binary: if done without a valid excuse (such as the need to clear phlegm for clarity or due to illness), it invalidates the prayer. If done with an excuse, it does not invalidate, and this is a unanimous ruling (bila khilaf). The excuse (udhr) is a condition that justifies the action, making it akin to an unavoidable sound. This ruling applies to all similar unavoidable sounds like coughing or sneezing.

Key Terms
تنحنح (tanahhun): Clearing the throat, producing a sound like 'Ahu'.
عذر (udhr): A valid excuse that justifies an action in prayer, such as illness or need to clear throat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whenever it produces a sound
B) Only if it is done without a valid excuse
C) Only if it is done loudly
D) It never invalidates prayer

Key Takeaway
Clearing the throat invalidates prayer only if done without a valid excuse; with excuse, the prayer remains valid.

The Hanafi Criterion for Sounds Invalidating Prayer
Definition of the ruling on sounds in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that only sounds with meaningful letters invalidate prayer, and apply it to distinguish invalidating speech from excusable sounds like groaning or animal calls.

Core Idea
Only sounds containing meaningful letters invalidate prayer; unavoidable sounds are excused.

The Hanafi school sets a precise criterion for what type of speech nullifies salah: it must consist of letters of the alphabet (hurūf al-hijā') that convey meaning, intended as human speech. This passage from Bahr al-Rā’iq expands on that principle, addressing disputes within the school and applying it to various scenarios like groaning, calling animals, or saying 'uff'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling criterion
Extract the principle from the text: sounds that invalidate prayer must contain letters of the alphabet and convey meaning (حروف الهجاء وإفهام المعنى). This is based on custom (ahlu al-'urf).
This distinguishes between human speech and mere sounds.
2
Map the intra-Hanafi dispute on extra letters
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad count even a single extra letter (e.g., the alif in 'yā') as speech if it forms at least two letters. Abu Yusuf does not count extra letters when few. The resolution from Fath al-Qadeer is that 'all letters' means at least two.
The thamarah al-khilaf: saying just 'yā' invalidates according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to Abu Yusuf.
3
Classify excusable sounds
Sounds that cannot be avoided (like groaning in pain, sneezing, belching) do not invalidate by consensus, even if they contain letters. Also, sounds without letters (calling animals) are not considered speech.
Crying without sound also does not invalidate.
4
Apply to edge cases in the text
Analyze examples: 'uff' (light vs. heavy forms), saying 'Allah' due to pain, reciting 'La hawla' for worldly reasons, or Quranic recitation for fever. Each is ruled based on intention and necessity.
Intention matters: if for worldly interaction, it invalidates; if for dhikr or unavoidable, it does not.
5
Distinguish the role of intention
The text shows that the same words can invalidate or not based on intention. For example, 'La hawla' for worldly reasons invalidates; for afterlife matters, it does not. And seeking refuge from whispers does not invalidate.
This aligns with the Hanafi maxim 'al-umūr bi-maqāṣidihā'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أهل العرف يتبع وجود حروف الهجاء وإفهام المعنى، ويتحقق ذلك في حروف كلها زوائد اهـ. وتعقبه الشارحون بأن أبا يوسف إنما يجعل حروف الزوائد كأن لم تكن إذا قلت لا إذا كثرت . وأجاب عنه في فتح القدير بأنه أراد بالجمع الاثنين فصاعداً. وجعل في الظهيرية محل الخلاف فيما إذا أمكن الامتناع عنه أما ما لا يمكن الامتناع عنه فلا يفسد عند الكل كالمريض إذا لم يملك نفسه من الأنين والتأوه لأنه حينئذ كالعطاس والجشأ إذا حصل بهما حروف قيد بالأنين ونحوه فإنه لو استعطف كلباً أو هرة أو ساق حماراً لم تفسد صلاته لأنه صوت لا هجاء له . وقيد بارتفاع بكائه لأنه لو خرج دمعه من غير صوت لا تفسد صلاته بلا خلاف في كل حال . كذا في شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان والتأفيف كالأنين كأف وتف . ثم «أف» اسم فعل لأتضجر، وقيل لتضجرت، وسواء أراد به تنقية موضع سجوده أو أراد به التأفيف فإن الصلاة تفسد عندهما مطلقاً. وقال أبو يوسف بعدمه، لكن في المجتبى : الصحيح أن خلافه إنما هو في المخفف وفي المشدد تفسد عندهم ويعارضه ما في الخلاصة أن الأصل عنده أن في الحرفين لا تفسد صلاته، وفي أربعة أحرف تفسد، وفي ثلاثة أحرف اختلف المشايخ فيها والأصح أنها لا تفسد اهـ. وبما فيها اندفع ما اعترض به الشارحون على الهداية في قوله ويتحقق ذلك في حروف كلها زوائد كما لا يخفى. وفي الخانية : ولو لدغته عقرب أو أصابه وجع فقال الله قال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل : تفسد صلاته ويكون بمنزلة الأنين . وهكذا روي عن أبي حنيفة. وقيل لا تفسد لأنه ليس من كلام الناس. وفي النصاب وعليه الفتوى وجزم به في الظهيرية، وكذا لو قال يا رب كما في الذخيرة. وفي الظهيرية ولو وسوسه الشيطان فقال لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله إن كان ذلك لأمر الآخرة لا تفسد وإن كان لأمر الدنيا تفسد خلافاً لأبي يوسف . ولو عود نفسه بشيء من القرآن للحمى ونحوها تفسد عندهم .اهـ. بخلاف التعوذ لدفع الوسوسة لا تفسد مطلقاً كما في القنية .
The people of custom follow the existence of letters of the alphabet and the conveyance of meaning, and that is realized in letters all of which are extra letters, end. The commentators objected that Abu Yusuf only considers extra letters as if they were not when they are few, not when they are many. And it was answered in Fath al-Qadeer that he meant by 'all' at least two. And it was placed in al-Zahiriyya that the place of disagreement is when it is possible to refrain; as for what cannot be refrained from, it does not invalidate according to all, like the sick person who cannot control himself from groaning and sighing because then it is like sneezing and belching if letters occur with them. He restricted with groaning and the like: for if one called a dog or cat or drove a donkey, his prayer is not invalidated because it is a sound without letters. And he restricted with the raising of his voice in crying: for if tears come out without sound, his prayer is not invalidated without disagreement in every case. Thus in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadikhan. And 'uff' is like groaning, like 'af' and 'taff'. Then 'af' is a verb noun meaning 'I am disgusted' or 'I became disgusted', and whether he intended to clean his prostration place or to express disgust, the prayer is invalidated according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) absolutely. Abu Yusuf said not. But in al-Mujtaba: the correct view is that his disagreement is only in the light form, but in the heavy form it invalidates according to them. And it is contradicted by what is in al-Khulasa that the principle with him is that with two letters his prayer is not invalidated, with four letters it is invalidated, and with three letters the mashayikh differed, and the most correct is that it is not invalidated, end. By this, what the commentators objected to al-Hidaya in his statement 'and that is realized in letters all of which are extra' is refuted, as is not hidden. And in al-Khaniyya: if a scorpion stung him or he was struck by pain and he said 'Allah', Sheikh Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: his prayer is invalidated and it is like groaning. Thus it is narrated from Abu Hanifa. And it was said it is not invalidated because it is not from the speech of people. In al-Nisab, and upon it is the fatwa, and it is affirmed in al-Zahiriyya, and likewise if he said 'Ya Rabb' as in al-Dhakhira. And in al-Zahiriyya: if the devil whispered to him and he said 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah', if that was for the matter of the afterlife it is not invalidated, and if for the matter of this world it is invalidated, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And if he accustomed himself to something from the Quran for fever and the like, it invalidates according to them, end. In contrast, seeking refuge to repel whispering does not invalidate at all as in al-Quniyya.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining what types of sounds invalidate prayer. The author begins by establishing the principle that the criterion for speech that invalidates prayer is the presence of letters of the alphabet (حروف الهجاء) that convey meaning, following the custom of speakers. He then addresses a dispute: Abu Yusuf does not count extra letters (حروف الزوائد) as speech when they are few, but Abu Hanifa counts them. The author cites Fath al-Qadeer's resolution that 'all letters' means at least two. He then gives examples: sounds that cannot be avoided (like groaning from illness) do not invalidate by consensus, like sneezing or belching if they contain letters. Calling animals or driving a donkey does not invalidate because the sound lacks letters. Crying without sound also does not invalidate. The text then discusses 'أف' (uff), noting that it invalidates prayer according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf differs in the light form. The author quotes various sources: al-Mujtaba, al-Khulasa, al-Khaniyya, al-Nisab, al-Dhakhira, al-Zahiriyya, and al-Quniyya, each providing specific rulings on sounds like saying 'Allah' in pain, saying 'La hawla' for worldly reasons, or reciting Quran for fever. The common thread is that speech with letters intended for worldly interaction (not for worship or unavoidable circumstances) invalidates prayer.

Key Terms
حروف الهجاء (ḥurūf al-hijā'): The letters of the alphabet that form meaningful words; sounds without these letters do not invalidate prayer.
كلام الناس (kalām al-nās): Speech of people, i.e., human language with meaning, as opposed to sounds like groaning or animal calls.
أنين (anīn): Groaning due to pain; if unavoidable, it does not invalidate prayer even if letters occur.
حروف الزوائد (ḥurūf al-zawāʾid): Extra letters that are not part of the root meaning of a word, e.g., the alif in 'yā'.
أف (uff): An expression of disgust; its utterance invalidates prayer according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but not according to Abu Yusuf in the light form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Clearing the throat with the sound 'Ahem' that contains letters.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines invalidating speech in prayer as any sound containing meaningful letters that is intentional and avoidable, with unavoidable sounds and those without letters being excused.

Answering a Sneezer Invalidates Prayer: Conditions and Exceptions
Expansion of the ruling on sneeze-response in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which responding to a sneeze invalidates prayer and derive the ruling from the hadith of Mu'awiya.

Core Idea
Responding to a sneeze with 'yarhamuka Allah' invalidates prayer unless said to oneself or for reward.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the concise matn ruling that answering a sneezer invalidates prayer. By examining the restrictions and exceptions, we see how intention and context determine whether a phrase is 'speech of people' that breaks prayer. Understanding these nuances is crucial for every practising Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The author states that saying 'yarhamuka Allah' in response to a sneeze invalidates the prayer. This is the default ruling.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam: 'This prayer of ours does not contain anything from the speech of people.' Responding to a sneeze is such speech.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala).
3
Identify the conditions for invalidation
Three conditions must be met: (1) it is a response (jawab) directed to another person, (2) the speaker intends the response (not hoping for reward), (3) according to the relied-upon view, the utterance must be composed of letters (muhajja).
If any condition is missing, the prayer is not invalidated.
4
Note the exceptions
The prayer is not invalidated if: the sneezer says it to himself (e.g., 'yarhamani Allah'), or says it hoping for reward without response intention, or says 'al-hamdu lillah' without intending a response.
Also, if the speaker intends to teach/ instruct, it does invalidate.
5
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some scholars do not require letters; they say any audible sound invalidates. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that letters are required, as per al-Khulasa.
This affects cases like making animal sounds in prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وجواب عاطس بيرحمك الله) أي يفسدها لأنه من كلام الناس ولهذا قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قاله وهو معاوية بن الحكم أن صلاتنا هذه لا فيها شيء من كلام الناس فجعل التشميت منه قيد بكونه جواباً لأنه لو قال العاطس لنفسه يرحمك الله يا نفسي لا تفسد لأنه لما لم [؟]سكن خطاباً لغيره لم يعتبر من كلام الناس كما إذا قال يرحمني الله . وقيد بقوله يرحمك الله لأنه لو قال العاطس أو السامع الحمد لله لا تفسد لأنه لم يتعارف جواباً وإن قصده وفيه اختلاف المشايخ، ومحله عند إرادة الجواب، أما إذا لم يرده بل قاله رجاء الثواب لا تفسد بالاتفاق كما في غاية البيان. ومحله أيضاً عند عدم إرادة التفهيم فلو أراده تفسد صلاة السامع القائل الحمد لله لأنه تعليم للغير من غير حاجة كما في منية المصلي وشرحها. وأشار المصنف بالجواب إلى أن المصلي لو عطس فقال له رجل يرحمك الله فقال العاطس آمين تفسد صلاته. ولهذا قال في الظهيرية. رجلان يصليان فعطس أحدهما فقال رجل خارج الصلاة يرحمك الله فقالا جميعاً آمين تفسد صلاة في الصلاة قوله : وبعض مشايخنا لم يشترطوا أي أن يكون مهجي بل الشرط كونه مسموعاً. وعبارة الفتح وبعضهم لا يشترط الحروف في الإفساد بعد كونه مسموعاً، وعلى هذا لو نفر طائراً أو دعاه فقوله حتى قيل إذا قال في صلاته ما يساق به الحمار لا تفسد الخ» تفريع على الأول إن كانت (لا) في قوله لا تفسد ثابتة في أصل جميع نسخ الظهيرية وإلا فهو تفريع على الثاني المتبادر. والذي رأيته فيما عندي من نسخة الظهيرية ثبوتها فتأمل قوله : (أي لم يجبه) ظاهره بما هو مسموع كما هو هـ.
His statement: (and answering a sneezer with 'may Allah have mercy on you') means it invalidates prayer because it is from the speech of people. For this reason, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said - and it was said by Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam - 'This prayer of ours does not contain anything from the speech of people.' Thus he considered sneezing (response) from it. He restricted by it being a response: because if the sneezer said to himself 'may Allah have mercy on you O myself', it does not invalidate, because since it was not an address to another, it is not considered from the speech of people, like when he says 'may Allah have mercy on me'. And he restricted by his saying 'yarhamuka Allah' because if the sneezer or the listener said 'al-hamdu lillah', it does not invalidate, because it is not conventionally a response, even if he intended it. And there is disagreement of the mashayikh on this. Its place is when intending the response; as for if he did not intend it but rather said it hoping for reward, it does not invalidate by agreement, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And its place also is when not intending instruction; if he intended instruction, the prayer of the listener who said 'al-hamdu lillah' is invalidated because it is teaching others without need, as in Munyat al-Musalli and its commentary. And the author (al-Musannif) indicated by 'response' that if a person praying sneezes, and a man says to him 'yarhamuka Allah', and the sneezer says 'amin', his prayer is invalidated. For this reason it is said in al-Zahiriyya: Two men are praying, one sneezes, a man outside the prayer says 'yarhamuka Allah', and both say 'amin', the prayer of the one in prayer is invalidated. His statement: 'and some of our mashayikh did not stipulate (letters)' i.e., that it must be composed of letters; rather the condition is that it is audible. And the expression of al-Fath: 'and some do not stipulate letters in invalidation after it being audible.' Upon this, if one scares a bird or calls it, his statement until it was said: 'if he says in his prayer that with which a donkey is driven, it does not invalidate' - this is a branch on the first view if the 'la' in 'la tafsud' is confirmed in all copies of al-Zahiriyya; otherwise it is a branch on the second, as is apparent. And what I saw in the copy of al-Zahiriyya that I have is its confirmation, so reflect. His statement: (i.e., he did not respond to him) its apparent meaning is with what is audible, as it is, end.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage explains the matn phrase '(وجواب عاطس بيرحمك الله)' meaning that responding to a sneeze with 'yarhamuka Allah' invalidates the prayer because it is considered the speech of people. The author cites the hadith of Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said that prayer does not contain any speech of people, thereby ruling that sneeze-response is such speech. However, the ruling is conditional: if the sneezer says it to himself (e.g., 'yarhamani Allah'), it does not invalidate because it is not addressed to another. Also, saying 'al-hamdu lillah' (praise to Allah) without intending it as a response does not invalidate, but there is a disagreement among mashayikh if it is intended as a response. The key distinction is intention: if said for reward (thawab) without intending response, it does not invalidate. If the person intends to instruct or teach (tafhim), then it invalidates the prayer of the speaker because it is teaching without need. The author then extends the ruling: if a worshipper sneezes, another says 'yarhamuka Allah', and the sneezer says 'amin', his prayer is invalidated. The text also discusses a general principle: some Hanafi scholars (like those who follow Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani) require that the sound must be composed of letters (مهجي) to invalidate, while others (like Sheikh Imam Kharzada) only require that it be audible. The author notes that the correct view (mufta bihi) is the first (letters condition), as supported by al-Khulasa. The last part of the passage is a textual note about a variant reading in al-Zahiriyya regarding the condition.

Key Terms
تشمت العاطس (tashmīt al-ʿāṭis): Responding to a sneezer by saying 'yarhamuka Allah' (may Allah have mercy on you).
جواب (jawāb): A response directed to another person; if said to oneself or without intention of response, it does not invalidate.
مهجي (muhajjā): Composed of letters of the alphabet; a condition for invalidation according to the preferred view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person praying sneezes and says 'al-hamdu lillah' to himself without intending to respond to anyone, what is the ruling?
A) His prayer is invalidated.
B) His prayer is not invalidated because he did not intend a response to another.
C) His prayer is invalidated only if he intends to teach others.
D) His prayer is invalidated because 'al-hamdu lillah' is not allowed in prayer.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'yarhamuka Allah' in prayer invalidates it only if it is a response to another person, intended as such, and (per the preferred view) composed of letters.
