Ruling on Clearing Throat in Prayer
Intra-Hanafi dispute on when clearing throat invalidates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position on when clearing the throat invalidates prayer by distinguishing between cases with and without distinct letters and valid purpose.

Core Idea
Clearing throat invalidates prayer only if it produces distinct letters without valid excuse or purpose.

Clearing the throat during prayer is a common action that raises a question: does it break the prayer? The Hanafi school has a nuanced position, distinguishing between cases based on whether the sound forms distinct letters and whether there is a valid purpose. This lesson unpacks the intra-madhhab dispute and establishes the mufta bihi ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Start with the base ruling: clearing the throat without distinct letters is makruh tanzih, not mufsid. This is agreed upon.
Refer to the phrase 'it is disliked' in the passage.
2
Recognize the condition for invalidation
If the clearing produces distinct letters (huruf muhajjah) and there is no valid excuse or purpose, it becomes mufsid (invalidates) according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
The passage says 'يفسد' when without excuse and purpose.
3
Analyze the exception for valid purpose
If the clearing is for a valid purpose (e.g., improving recitation, signaling), it does not invalidate even with letters. This is the mu'tamad view.
The passage quotes Fath al-Qadir supporting non-invalidation with purpose.
4
Explore the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on audibility vs. letters
Some mashayikh (e.g., Halwani) require letters for invalidation; others (e.g., Kharzadah) consider any audible sound sufficient. The mu'tamad requires letters.
The passage reports the opinion of Kharzadah: 'إذا قال في صلاته ما يساق به الحمار لا تفسد إذا لم يحصل به الحروف'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon view is that clearing throat invalidates only if it produces distinct letters without valid excuse or purpose. This is supported by al-Khulasa and Fath al-Qadir.
Consequence: a person who clears throat to improve recitation does not invalidate prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
به حروف لأنه جاء من قبل من له الحق فجعل عفواً، وإن كان من غير عذر ولا غرض صحيح فهو مفسد عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف في الحرفين، وإن كان بغير عذر لكن لغرض صحيح كتحسين صوته للقراءة أو للإعلام أنه في الصلاة أو ليهتدي إمامه عند خطائه ففيه اختلاف؛ فظاهر الكتاب والظهيرية اختيار الفساد لكن الصحيح عدمه لأن ما للقراءة ملحق بها كما في فتح القدير وغيره فلو قال بلا عذر وغرض صحيح لكان أولى إلا أن يستعمل العذر فيما هو أعم من المضطر إليه قيدنا بأن يظهر له حروف لأنه لو لم يظهر له حروف مهجاة فإنه لا يفسدها اتفاقاً لكنه مكروه وهو محمل قول من قال إن التنحنح قصداً واختياراً مكروه لأنه عبث لعروه عن الفائدة وقيد بالتنحنح لأنه لو تثاءب فحصل منه صوت أو عطس فحصل منه صوت مع الحروف لا تفسد صلاته كذا في الظهيرية. ثم قال: التنحنح في الصلاة إن لم يكن مسموعاً لا تفسد، وإن كان مسموعاً يفسد. ظن بعض مشايخنا أن المسموع ما يكون مهجى نحو أف وتف وغير المسموع ما لا يكون مهجى إلى هذا مال شمس الأئمة الحلواني. وبعض مشايخنا لم يشترطوا وإليه مال الشيخ الإمام خواهر زاده حتى قيل: إذا قال في صلاته ما يساق به الحمار لا تفسد إذا لم يحصل به الحروف اهـ. واختار الأول صاحب الخلاصة وذكر أنه إذا لم يفسد فهو مكروه
With letters, because it came from one who has the right, so it is excused. If it is without excuse and no valid purpose, it invalidates according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf regarding two letters. If without excuse but for a valid purpose like improving his voice for recitation or to indicate he is in prayer or to guide his imam when he errs, there is disagreement: the apparent of the book and al-Zahiriyyah choose invalidity, but the correct is it does not invalidate because what is for recitation is attached to it as in Fath al-Qadir etc. So if he said without excuse and valid purpose it would be better unless excuse is used in what is more general than compelled. We restricted that letters appear because if no distinct letters appear, it does not invalidate by agreement, but it is disliked. This is the interpretation of the one who said clearing throat intentionally and deliberately is disliked because it is frivolous due to lack of benefit. It was restricted to clearing throat because if he yawned and a sound came or sneezed and a sound came with letters, his prayer is not invalidated, as in al-Zahiriyyah. Then he said: Clearing throat in prayer if not audible does not invalidate, and if audible it invalidates. Some of our mashayikh thought that audible is what has letters like 'uff' and 'taff', and inaudible is what has no letters. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani leaned to this. Some of our mashayikh did not require that, and Shaykh Imam Kharzadah leaned to them until it was said: If he says in his prayer what is used to drive a donkey, it does not invalidate if no letters are produced. The author of al-Khulasa chose the first and mentioned that if it does not invalidate, it is disliked.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh discusses the ruling on clearing the throat (tanahnuh) during prayer. It begins by categorizing situations: if the sound comes with letters (huruf) due to a valid excuse (like inability to avoid), it is excused and does not invalidate. If it is without excuse and without valid purpose, it invalidates according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), contrary to Abu Yusuf's earlier view regarding two letters (a minor ikhtilaf within his own position). If it is without excuse but for a valid purpose like improving recitation or signaling, there is disagreement: some texts indicate invalidity, but the correct position (sahih) is that it does not invalidate because such actions are auxiliary to the prayer. The author quotes several sources: the zahir of the matn and al-Zahiriyyah choose invalidity, but Fath al-Qadir supports non-invalidation. The text then explains that the ruling depends on whether distinct letters (huru' hijaiyyah) appear. By agreement, if no letters appear, it does not invalidate but is disliked. This is the basis for the view that intentional clearing of throat is disliked. The author distinguishes clearing throat from yawning or sneezing, which do not invalidate even if they produce sounds and letters. The passage then reports a difference among the mashayikh: some require that the sound be with letters for invalidation, while others do not require letters, only audibility. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani preferred the former, while Shaykh Imam Kharzadah preferred the latter. The author of al-Khulasa chose the first view (that letters are necessary) and added that if it does not invalidate, it remains disliked.

Key Terms
التنحنح (al-tanahnuh): Clearing the throat, producing a sound from the throat with or without letters.
مفسد (mufsid): An act that invalidates the prayer entirely, requiring repetition.
حروف مهجاة (huruf muhajjah): Distinct Arabic letters or syllables that can be spelled out, like 'af' or 'taf'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the more correct Hanafi view, when does clearing the throat invalidate prayer?
A) Whenever it produces any audible sound.
B) Only if it produces distinct letters and there is no valid excuse or purpose.
C) Only if it is done intentionally regardless of sound.
D) It never invalidates prayer.

Key Takeaway
Clearing throat in prayer only invalidates if distinct letters are produced without a valid excuse or purpose; otherwise it is merely disliked.

Responding to Sneeze and Saying 'Ameen' in Prayer
Ruling on saying 'Yarhamukallah' and 'Ameen' during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on responding to a sneezer and saying 'Ameen' during prayer, and apply the key condition of intention to determine invalidation.

Core Idea
Responding to a sneezer or saying 'Ameen' invalidates prayer if intended as speech; without response intent, it does not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies when certain utterances invalidate prayer. The key factor is niyyah (intention): whether the speaker intended to respond to another person (jawab) or simply to supplicate or praise Allah. The hadith of Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam establishes that prayer is free from human speech, but intent distinguishes valid from invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is responding to a sneezer by saying 'Yarhamukallah'. The ruling is that it invalidates prayer.
The passage explicitly states 'أي يفسدها لأنه من كلام الناس'.
2
Locate the Dalil
What proof is cited? The hadith of Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam where the Prophet said: 'In this prayer of ours, nothing of people's speech is allowed.' This categorizes responding as speech of people.
The hadith is a strong proof, though zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the Condition of Intention
The ruling hinges on whether the utterance is intended as a response (jawab). If the person says it without response intent (e.g., hoping for reward), it does not invalidate. Intent is pivotal.
The passage distinguishes: 'قيد بكونه جواباً' and 'محله عند إرادة الجواب'.
4
Note Expansions to Similar Cases
The passage applies the same principle to saying 'Ameen' in response to a du'a, responding to adhan, sending blessings on the Prophet, and saying 'Labbayka'. All invalidate if intended as response.
Even saying 'Alhamdulillah' can invalidate if intended to teach.
5
Identify the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf initially allowed moving tongue softly for praising Allah after sneezing, then retracted and forbade any movement. The final position is silence.
This shows the school's caution: even internal movement may be avoided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وجواب عاطس بيرحمك الله) أي يفسدها لأنه من كلام الناس ولهذا قال النبي الله للقائله وهو معاوية بن الحكم أن صلاتنا هذه لا فيها شيء من كلام الناس فجعل التشميت منه قيد بكونه جواباً لأنه لو قال العاطس لنفسه يرحمك الله يا نفسي لا تفسد لأنه لما لم يكن خطاباً لغيره لم يعتبر من كلام الناس كما إذا قال يرحمني الله. وقيد بقوله يرحمك الله لأنه لو قال العاطس أو السامع الحمد لله لا تفسد لأنه لم يتعارف جواباً وإن قصده وفيه اختلاف المشايخ، ومحله عند إرادة الجواب، أما إذا لم يرده بل قاله رجاء الثواب لا تفسد بالاتفاق كا في غاية البيان. ومحله أيضاً عند عدم إرادة التفهيم فلو أراده تفسد صلاة السامع القائل الحمد لله لأنه تعليم للغير من غير حاجة كما في منية المصلي وشرحها. وأشار المصنف بالجواب إلى أن المصلي لو عطس فقال له رجل يرحمك الله فقال العاطس آمين تفسد صلاته. ولهذا قال في الظهيرية. رجلان يصليان فعطس أحدهما فقال رجل خارج الصلاة يرحمك الله فقالا جميعاً آمين تفسد صلاة العاطس ولا تفسد صلاة الآخر لأنه لم يدع له .اهـ. أي لم يجبه ويشكل عليه ما في الذخيرة إذا أمن المصلي لدعاء رجل ليس في الصلاة تفسد صلاته .اهـ. وهو يفيد فساد صلاة المؤمن الذي ليس بعاطس وليس ببعيد كما لا يخفي وأشار إلى أن المصلي إذا سمع الأذان فقال مثل ما يقول المؤذن، إن أراد جوابه تفسد وإلا فلا. وإن لم تكن له نية تفسد لأن الظاهر أنه أراد به الإجابة، وكذلك إذا سمع اسم النبي الله فصلى عليه فهذا إجابة فتفسد، وإن صلى عليه ولم يسمع اسمه لا تفسد ولو قال لبيك سيدي حين قرأ يا أيها الذين آمنوا ففيه قولان يسمع والأحسن أن لا يفعل كذا في المحيط. وفي الذخيرة معزياً إلى نوادر بشر عن أبي يوسف أنه إذا عطس الرجل في الصلاة حمد الله، فإن كان وحده فإن شاء أسر به وحرك لسانه، وإن شاء أعلن، وإن كان خلف إمام أسر به وحرك لسانه، ثم رجع أبو يوسف وقال: لا يحرك لسانه مطلقاً .اهـ. وهو متعين ولهذا قال في الخلاصة : وينبغي أن يقول في نفسه والأحسن هو السكوت. وفي القنية مسجد كبير يجهر المؤذن فيه بالتكبيرات فدخل فيه رجل نادى المؤذن أن يجهر بالتكبير فرفع الإمام للحال وجهر المؤذن بالتكبير، فإن قصد جوابه فسدت صلاته، وكذا لو قال عند ختم الإمام قراءته صدق الله وصدق الرسول وكذا إذا ذكر في تشهده الشهادتين عند ذكر المؤذن الشهادتين تفسد إن قصد الإجابة اهـ.
His statement: (And responding to a sneezer with 'Yarhamukallah') means it invalidates because it is from the speech of people. For this reason the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to the one who said it, Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam, 'In this prayer of ours, nothing of people's speech is allowed.' So he made tasmit from it. It is restricted to it being a response because if the sneezer said to himself 'Yarhamukallah O my soul', it does not invalidate because since it was not addressed to another, it is not considered from people's speech, just as if he said 'Yarhamani Allah'. And it is restricted to saying 'Yarhamukallah' because if the sneezer or the hearer said 'Alhamdulillah', it does not invalidate because it is not conventionally a response, even if he intended it, and there is disagreement of the mashayikh. And its place is when intending response; if he did not intend it but said it hoping for reward, it does not invalidate by agreement, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And its place is also when not intending to teach; if he intended teaching, the prayer of the hearer who said 'Alhamdulillah' invalidates because it is teaching another without need, as in Munyat al-Musalli and its commentary. And the author indicated by 'response' that if a person in prayer sneezes and a man says 'Yarhamukallah', and the sneezer says 'Ameen', his prayer invalidates. Hence it is said in al-Zahiriyyah: Two men praying, one sneezes, a man outside prayer says 'Yarhamukallah', and both say 'Ameen' – the prayer of the sneezer invalidates, but not the other's because he (the sneezer) did not supplicate for him. Meaning he did not respond to him. And it is problematic from al-Dhakhira: If a person in prayer says 'Ameen' to the supplication of a man not in prayer, his prayer invalidates. This implies invalidation of the prayer of the one who is not the sneezer, which is not far-fetched. And he indicated that if a person in prayer hears the adhan and says similar to what the mu'adhdhin says, if he intends response it invalidates, otherwise not. If he has no intention, it invalidates because apparently he intended response. Similarly, if he hears the name of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and sends blessings on him, this is response so it invalidates. If he sends blessings without hearing the name, it does not invalidate. If he says 'Labbayka sayyidi' when reciting 'O you who believe', there are two opinions, the best is not to do it, as in al-Muhit. And in al-Dhakhira, narrated from Nawadir Bishr from Abu Yusuf that if a man sneezes in prayer and praises Allah, if he is alone, he may say it softly and move his tongue, or aloud; if behind an imam, he says it softly and moves his tongue. Then Abu Yusuf retracted and said: he should not move his tongue at all. This is definite, hence in al-Khulasa: He should say it to himself, and the best is silence. In al-Qunyah: A large mosque where the mu'adhdhin says the takbirs loudly; a man entered and asked the mu'adhdhin to say the takbir loudly; the imam immediately raised and the mu'adhdhin said the takbir loudly. If he intended response, his prayer invalidates. Similarly, if he says at the end of the imam's recitation 'Sadaqa Allah wa sadaqa rasuluh', and similarly if he mentions the two shahadahs in his tashahhud when the mu'adhdhin mentions them, it invalidates if he intends response.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on responding to a sneezer (tashmit al-atis) and saying 'Ameen' during prayer. It begins by stating that saying 'Yarhamukallah' to a sneezer invalidates prayer because it is considered speech of people. The proof is the hadith of Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam. The author emphasizes that it only invalidates if it is a response (jawab) to the sneeze; if the sneezer says it to himself, it does not invalidate because it is not addressed to another. Similarly, saying 'Alhamdulillah' by the sneezer or hearer does not invalidate because it is not conventionally a response, though there is disagreement among scholars. The ruling depends on intent: if one intends response, it invalidates; if one intends reward only, it does not. However, if one intends to teach the other person (like saying 'Alhamdulillah' to teach him to praise Allah), it invalidates because it becomes teaching. The passage also covers the case where a sneezer in prayer hears 'Yarhamukallah' and says 'Ameen' – this invalidates the sneezer's prayer but not that of a fellow worshipper who also says 'Ameen' because the response is only for the one supplicated for (the sneezer). There is a conflicting report from al-Dhakhira suggesting invalidation for both, but the author reconciles. The text then extends to similar situations: responding to adhan, sending blessings on the Prophet, saying 'Labbayka' – all invalidate if intended as response. For praising Allah after sneezing, Abu Yusuf's final view is that one should not even move the tongue; the recommended practice is silence. The passage ends with various scenarios from al-Qunyah about responding to takbirs and shahadahs, emphasizing that intent is key.

Key Terms
تشميت العاطس (tashmit al-atis): Responding to a sneezer by saying 'Yarhamukallah' (may Allah have mercy on you).
جواب (jawab): A direct response to another's speech, distinct from mere invocation or recitation.
آمين (ameen): A supplicatory word meaning 'O Allah, accept'; considered response to a supplication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person praying hears the mu'adhdhin and says the same words as the adhan, when does it invalidate his prayer?
A) Always invalidates.
B) Only if he intends to respond.
C) Never invalidates.
D) Only if he says it loudly.

Key Takeaway
The critical determinant for invalidation of prayer by speech is intention: if the utterance is intended as a response (jawab) to another person, it invalidates; if it is mere supplication or remembrance without response intent, it does not.

When Does Saying Ameen Invalidate Prayer? The Sneezer Case and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on when saying Ameen invalidates prayer, with sneezer case as primary example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule that saying Ameen invalidates prayer only when it is a direct response (jawab) to a supplication addressed to the speaker, and apply this rule to the sneezer case and other scenarios.

Core Idea
Saying Ameen in prayer invalidates only when it is a direct response to a supplication made specifically to you.

Have you ever wondered whether saying 'Ameen' in prayer could break your salah? The Hanafi school holds that 'Ameen' itself is not problematic—it only invalidates when it functions as a 'jawab' (response) to someone else's supplication. The classic case is the sneezer: when a praying person sneezes and another says 'Yarhamukallah', the sneezer's 'Ameen' is a response and invalidates his prayer. But what if another praying person also says 'Ameen'? The scholars differ, and this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq walks through the reasoning and the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The passage establishes that saying 'Ameen' (ta’min) in prayer does not inherently invalidate. The invalidation occurs only if the 'Ameen' constitutes a response (jawab) to an external supplication. You need to first understand this baseline.
2
Apply to the sneezer scenario
When a praying person sneezes, another says 'Yarhamukallah'. The sneezer's 'Ameen' is a direct response to that supplication because he is the one being prayed for. Therefore, his prayer becomes invalid (fasid). This is the clear case.
Also note that the person who said 'Yarhamukallah' may have his own ruling if he is also praying.
3
Analyze the non-sneezer's 'Ameen'
If another person in prayer also says 'Ameen', the majority of Hanafi scholars rule that his prayer is not invalidated because the supplication was not addressed to him. His 'Ameen' is not a response (jawab). This is supported by Qadikhan's example.
However, some scholars like those cited in al-Dhakhira consider any 'Ameen' to any supplication as invalidating, creating a dispute.
4
Resolve the intra-madhhab dispute
The passage reconciles the al-Dhakhira view by noting that it applies when the supplication is intended for the listener. Al-Maqdisi explains: if the supplication is for the person saying 'Ameen', then it is a response; if not, it is not. This resolves the apparent contradiction.
The author prefers al-Maqdisi's interpretation over al-Nahr's.
5
Extend the principle to other cases
The passage also applies the same principle to responding to adhan, sending salawat upon hearing the Prophet's name, and saying 'Labbayk'. In each case, if the praying person intends to respond, it invalidates; otherwise, it does not. The later scholars (mutakkhirin) extend this to saying 'Ameen' after 'wala al-dallin'.
This shows the principle is consistently applied across various scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاطس ولا تفسد صلاة الآخر لأنه لم يدع له .اهـ. أي لم يجبه ويشكل عليه ما في الذخيرة إذا أمن المصلي لدعاء رجل ليس في الصلاة تفسد صلاته .اهـ. وهو يفيد فساد صلاة المؤمن الذي ليس بعاطس وليس ببعيد كما لا يخفي وأشار إلى أن المصلي إذا سمع الأذان فقال مثل ما يقول المؤذن، إن أراد جوابه تفسد وإلا فلا. وإن لم تكن له نية تفسد لأن الظاهر أنه أراد به الإجابة، وكذلك إذا سمع اسم النبي الله فصلى عليه فهذا إجابة فتفسد، وإن صلى عليه ولم اسمه لا تفسد ولو قال لبيك سيدي حين قرأ يا أيها الذين آمنوا ففيه قولان يسمع والأحسن أن لا يفعل كذا في المحيط. وفي الذخيرة معزياً إلى نوادر بشر عن أبي يوسف أنه إذا عطس الرجل في الصلاة حمد الله، فإن كان وحده فإن شاء أسر به وحرك لسانه، وإن شاء أعلن، وإن كان خلف إمام أسر به وحرك لسانه، ثم رجع أبو يوسف وقال: لا يحرك لسانه مطلقاً .اهـ. وهو متعين ولهذا قال في الخلاصة : وينبغي أن يقول في نفسه والأحسن هو السكوت. وفي القنية مسجد كبير يجهر المؤذن فيه بالتكبيرات فدخل فيه رجل نادى المؤذن أن يجهر بالتكبير فرفع الإمام للحال وجهر المؤذن بالتكبير، فإن قصد جوابه فسدت صلاته، وكذا لو قال عند ختم الإمام قراءته صدق الله وصدق الرسول وكذا إذا ذكر في تشهده الشهادتين عند ذكر المؤذن الشهادتين تفسد إن قصد الإجابة اهـ . . كذلك أن الضمير المنصوب في قوله «لأنه لم يدع له عائد إلى المصلي الآخر، والأظهر أنه عائد إلى الرجل الخارج أي لأن القائل يرحمك الله إنما دعا بذلك للعاطس لا للمصلي الآخر فكان قول العاطس آمين جواباً للداعي له بخلاف المصلي الآخر فلم يكن تأمينه جواباً له تأمل قوله : (وهو يفيد فساد صلاة المؤمن الذي ليس بعاطس) قال في النهر : لا نسلم أن الثاني تأمين لدعائه لانقطاعه بالأول وإلى هذا يشير التعليل ا هـ. أي التعليل بأنه لم يجبه فإنه يفيد أن الإجابة حصلت بتأمين العاطس فلم يكن الثاني تأميناً لدعائه وكلام الذخيرة فيه فليتأمل وفي شرح نظم الكنز للعلامة المقدسي أن ما في الذخيرة محمول على ما إذا دعا له ليكون جواباً، أما إذا دعا لغيره فلا يظهر كونه جواباً فلا تفسد ا هـ . وهو أولى مما في النهر. والحاصل أن التأمين في نفسه غير مفسد، وإنما يفسد إذا كان جواباً وهو في مسألة الذخيرة بناء على أن المراد الدعاء للمصلي بخلاف ما في الظهيرية لأن الجواب إنما يكون من المدعو له وهو العاطس فقط، فتأمينه مفسد بخلاف تأمين الآخر. ويوضح هذا ما . عن قاضيخان: لو عطس المصلي فقال له رجل يرحمك الله فقال المصلي آمين فسدت صلاته لأنه أجابه، ولو قال من بجنبه معه أيضاً آمين لا تفسد صلاته لأن تأمينه ليس بجواب ا هـ . والمراد بمن بجنبه أي من المصلين بدليل قوله لا تفسد صلاته لكن سيأتي بعد نحو ورقة عن المبتغي: لو سمع المصلي من مصل آخر ولا الضالين فقال آمين لا تفسد ، وقيل تفسد وعليه المتأخرون فليتأمل
The sneezer: and the prayer of the other is not invalidated because he did not supplicate for him. That is, he did not answer him. And it is objected by what is in al-Dhakhira: if a person praying says Ameen to the supplication of a man not in prayer, his prayer is invalidated. And it indicates the invalidation of the prayer of the believer who is not a sneezer, and it is not far-fetched as is not hidden. And he indicated that if a praying person hears the adhan and says like what the mu'adhdhin says, if he intended to respond, it invalidates; otherwise not. And if he has no intention, it invalidates because the apparent is that he intended by it the response. Similarly, if he hears the name of the Prophet (ﷺ) and sends salawat upon him, this is a response so it invalidates; and if he sends salawat without hearing his name, it does not invalidate. And if he says 'Labbayk, master' when reciting 'O you who have believed' there are two opinions: it is heard, and the best is not to do it. Thus in al-Muhit. And in al-Dhakhira, attributed to Nawadir Bishr from Abu Yusuf, that if a man sneezes in prayer and praises Allah, if he is alone, he may either conceal it and move his tongue, or do it openly; and if he is behind an imam, he conceals it and moves his tongue. Then Abu Yusuf retracted and said: he should not move his tongue at all. This is established, and for this reason it is said in al-Khulasa: he should say it in himself, and it is best to be silent. And in al-Qunyah: a large mosque where the mu'adhdhin recites the takbirs loudly, and a man entered and called to the mu'adhdhin to recite the takbirs loudly, and the imam immediately raised (his head) and the mu'adhdhin recited the takbirs loudly; if he intended to respond, his prayer is invalidated. Likewise, if he says when the imam finishes his recitation 'Allah spoke the truth and the Messenger spoke the truth'; likewise if he mentions in his tashahhud the two testimonies when the mu'adhdhin mentions them, it invalidates if he intended response. So also: the accusative pronoun in his statement 'because he did not supplicate for him' refers to the other person praying, but the more apparent is that it refers to the external man, because the one saying 'may Allah have mercy on you' only made that supplication for the sneezer, not for the other person praying. Thus the sneezer's saying 'Ameen' was a response to the supplicant for him, unlike the other person praying, so his 'Ameen' was not a response to him. Consider his statement: 'and it indicates the invalidation of the prayer of the believer who is not a sneezer.' He said in al-Nahr: we do not accept that the second is a 'Ameen' to his supplication because it was cut off by the first; and this is indicated by the reasoning. That is, the reasoning that he did not answer him indicates that the response was accomplished by the sneezer's 'Ameen', so the second was not 'Ameen' to his supplication. And the statement of al-Dhakhira requires consideration. And in Sharh Nazm al-Kanz by 'Allama al-Maqdisi, what is in al-Dhakhira is interpreted as when (the supplication) was made to him to be a response; as for when it is for someone else, it does not appear to be a response, so it does not invalidate. This is better than what is in al-Nahr. The conclusion is that 'Ameen' in itself is not invalidating; it only invalidates if it is a response. And in the case of al-Dhakhira, it is based on the assumption that the supplication was intended for the person praying, unlike what is in al-Zahirah, because the response is only from the one supplicated for, who is the sneezer only, so his 'Ameen' invalidates, unlike the 'Ameen' of another. And this is clarified by what Qadikhan said: if a praying person sneezes and a man says to him 'may Allah have mercy on you', and the sneezer says 'Ameen', his prayer is invalidated because he answered him; and if someone beside him also says 'Ameen', his prayer is not invalidated because his 'Ameen' is not a response. The intended meaning of 'someone beside him' is those praying with him, as evidenced by his statement 'his prayer is not invalidated'. But it will come later in about a folio from al-Mubtaghi: if a praying person hears from another worshipper 'nor those who go astray' and says 'Ameen', it does not invalidate; and it is said it invalidates, and the later scholars adopt this. So reflect.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the invalidation of prayer by saying 'Ameen'. The core principle established is that 'Ameen' itself does not invalidate prayer; it only does so when it constitutes a response (jawab) to an external supplication. The discussion centers on the sneezer case: if a person sneezes and another says 'Yarhamukallah', the sneezer's 'Ameen' is a response and invalidates his prayer. However, if another praying person also says 'Ameen', it does not invalidate because the supplication was directed at the sneezer alone. This is supported by Qadikhan's example. An objection from al-Dhakhira claims that if a praying person says 'Ameen' to any supplication (even for another), it invalidates. The author reconciles this by noting that in al-Dhakhira's scenario, the supplication was intended for the person praying. Al-Maqdisi's interpretation is preferred: only if the supplication is directly for the hearer does 'Ameen' become a response. The passage also covers related issues: responding to the adhan, sending salawat upon hearing the Prophet's name, and saying 'Labbayk' during prayer. For adhan, if the praying person intends to respond by repeating the mu'adhdhin's words, it invalidates; without intention, it also invalidates due to presumption of response. For salawat, if the praying person hears the Prophet's name and sends salawat, it is a response and invalidates; otherwise not. The case of 'Labbayk' when hearing 'Ya ayyuha alladhina amanu' has two opinions. Abu Yusuf's earlier view allowed moving the tongue for internal remembrance in prayer, but he later retracted, and the preferred position is to remain silent. The later scholars (mutakkhirin) hold that saying 'Ameen' in response to 'wala al-dallin' invalidates, and this is the operative fatwa.

Key Terms
تأمين (ta’min): The act of saying 'Ameen' in prayer.
جواب (jawab): A direct response to another person's speech or supplication.
عاطس (atis): A person who sneezes; the one for whom the supplication 'Yarhamukallah' is made.
فساد (fasad): Invalidation of the prayer; the prayer becomes void and must be repeated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a praying person hears someone else sneeze and says 'Yarhamukallah', and then another person (also in prayer) says 'Ameen', does the second person's prayer become invalid according to the majority?
A) Yes, because any 'Ameen' in prayer invalidates it.
B) Yes, because the 'Ameen' is a response to the supplication.
C) No, because the supplication was directed at the sneezer, not at him, so his 'Ameen' is not a response.
D) No, because saying 'Ameen' is always permissible in prayer.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'Ameen' in prayer only invalidates when it is a direct response (jawab) to an external supplication addressed specifically to the speaker; the sneezer case illustrates this, and the majority Hanafi view restricts invalidation to that scenario.

The Ruling on Prompting (Fath) During Prayer: Imam vs. Others
Ruling on prompting (fath) in prayer with proof and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prompting the imam versus prompting a non-imam in prayer, apply the condition of intent to determine invalidity, and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute regarding frequency of prompting.

Core Idea
Prompting the imam is permissible; prompting a non-imam invalidates prayer if done to teach.

In prayer, one may need to complete a verse for another reciter. The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between prompting the imam—which perfects the prayer—and prompting any other person, which is akin to teaching and invalidates the prayer if done with that intent. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the basis for this distinction, the proof from the hadith of 'Ali (رضي الله عنه), and the positions of later mashayikh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its two contexts
First, recognize that 'fath' (prompting) is completing a verse for someone else in prayer. The ruling depends on whether the prompted person is the imam or someone else (e.g., a fellow muqtadi or a person praying alone).
The matn focuses on 'prompting someone other than his imam' – this is the default case.
2
Extract the ruling for each context
For the imam: the text says 'there is no invalidation' (la fasad) and quotes the hadith 'When the imam seeks feeding, feed him'. For a non-imam: 'it invalidates it because it is teaching and learning without need.' Note that intent matters: if one intends to teach, it invalidates; if one intends merely to recite Quran, it does not.
The ruling for the imam is absolute: his prayer and the prompter's prayer remain valid. For a non-imam, invalidation depends on the intention (nīyah).
3
Examine the proof from 'Ali (رضي الله عنه)
The proof for permitting prompting the imam is the statement of 'Ali: 'When the imam seeks feeding, feed him, and his seeking feeding is his silence.' This indicates that the imam's silence after a pause is a request for prompting, and responding is a form of assistance, not a violation of prayer.
This hadith is cited with the term 'istit'ām' (seeking food) as a metaphor for needing prompting. The command 'fa-at'imūhu' (feed him) is taken as a permission.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreements and the mufta bihi
The text discusses ikhtilaf on two points: (a) whether the imam's prayer invalidates if he takes a prompt after moving to another verse (the correct view is it does not); (b) whether prompting a non-imam invalidates with one instance or requires repetition (the relied-upon view is that any instance invalidates, contrary to al-Bada'i'). The mufta bihi is that prompting the imam never invalidates, and prompting a non-imam invalidates if done with teaching intent, regardless of frequency.
Al-Bada'i's distinction (one instance invalidates only after opening, otherwise repetition) is explicitly rejected as 'contrary to the madhhab'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأشار إلى أن المصلي إذا سمع الأذان الخ) أدخل في النهر هذه الفروع تحت قوله «والجواب بلا إله إلا الله» قال: وما سلكناه أولى قوله : ( وفتحه على غير إمامه أي يفسدها لأنه تعليم وتعلم لغير حاجة . قيد به لأنه لو فتح على إمامه فلا فساد لأنه تعلق به إصلاح صلاته أما إن كان الإمام لم يقرأ الفرض فظاهر، وأما إن كان قرأ ففيه اختلاف والصحيح عدم الفساد لأنه لو لم يفتح ربما يجري على لسانه ما يكون مفسداً فكان فيه إصلاح صلاته ولاطلاق ما روي عن علي رضي الله عنه إذا استطعمكم الإمام فأطعموه واستطعامه سكوته . ولهذا لو فتح على إمامه بعدما انتقل إلى آية أخرى لا تفسد صلاته وهو قول عامة المشايخ لإطلاق المرخص. وفي المحيط ما يفيد أنه المذهب فإن فيه وذكر في الأصل والجامع الصغير أنه إذا فتح على إمامه يجوز مطلقاً لأن الفتح وإن كان تعليماً ولكن التعليم ليس بعمل كثير وأنه تلاوة حقيقة فلا يكون مفسداً وإن لم يكن محتاجاً إليه وصحح في الظهيرية أنه لا تفسد صلاة الفاتح على كل حال وتفسد صلاة الإمام إذا أخذ من الفاتح بعدما انتقل إلى آية أخرى. وصحح المصنف في الكافي أنه لا تفسد صلاة الإمام أيضاً، فصار الحاصل أن الصحيح من المذهب أن الفتح على إمامه لا صلاة أحد، لا الفاتح ولا الآخذ مطلقاً في كل حال. ثم قيل: ينوي الفاتح بالفتح على إمامه التلاوة، والصحيح أنه نوي الفتح دون القراءة لأن قراءة المقتدي منهي عنها والفتح على إمامه عنه. قالوا : يكره للمقتدي أن يفتح على إمامه من ساعته، وكذا يكره للإمام أن يلجئهم إليه بأن يقف ساكتاً بعد الحصر أو يكرر الآية بل يركع إذا جاء أوانه أو ينتقل إلى آية أخرى لم يلزم من وصلها ما يفسد الصلاة أو ينتقل إلى سورة أخرى كما في المحيط . واختلفت الرواية في وقت أوان الركوع ففي بعضها اعتبر أوانه المستحب، وفي بعضها اعتبر فرض القراءة يعني إذا قرأ مقدار ما تجوز به الصلاة ركع. كذا في السراج الوهاج. وأراد من الفتح على غير إمامه تلقينه على قصد التعليم، أما إن قصد قراءة القرآن فلا تفسد عند الكل. كذا في الخلاصة وغيرها. وأطلق في الفتح المذكور فشمل ما إذا تكرر منه أو كان مرة واحدة His statement: 'And he indicated that if the praying person hears the adhan...' He included in al-Nahr these branches under his statement 'and the response with la ilaha illallah'. He said: what we have followed is better. His statement: 'And his prompting someone other than his imam' means it invalidates it because it is teaching and learning without need. He restricted it because if he prompts his imam, there is no invalidation because it pertains to correcting his prayer. As for if the imam has not yet recited the obligatory (verse), it is apparent; and if he has recited, there is disagreement, and the correct is that it does not invalidate because if he does not prompt, perhaps something invalidating might come upon his tongue, so there is correction of his prayer in it, and due to the general application of what was narrated from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'When the imam seeks feeding, feed him; and his seeking feeding is his silence.' For this reason, if he prompts his imam after he has moved to another verse, his prayer does not invalidate, and this is the statement of the generality of the mashayikh due to the general application of the license. And in al-Muhit, what indicates that it is the madhhab: that in al-Asl and al-Jami' al-Saghir, if he prompts his imam, it is allowed absolutely because the prompting, even if it is teaching, is not a lot of action, and it is truly recitation, so it does not invalidate even if he is not in need of it. And it was authenticated in al-Zahirah that the prayer of the one prompting does not invalidate in all cases, and the prayer of the imam invalidates if he takes from the prompter after moving to another verse. And the author authenticated in al-Kafi that the prayer of the imam also does not invalidate. So the result is that the correct from the madhhab is that prompting the imam does not invalidate the prayer of anyone, neither the prompter nor the taker, absolutely in all cases. Then it was said: the prompter intends by prompting his imam recitation, but the correct is that he intends the prompting without recitation because the recitation of the follower is prohibited, and prompting his imam is permitted. They said: it is disliked for the follower to prompt his imam immediately, and likewise it is disliked for the imam to force them to do so by remaining silent after being stuck or repeating the verse, but rather he should bow when its time comes, or move to another verse that, by connecting it, does not invalidate the prayer, or move to another surah, as in al-Muhit. And the reports differ about the time of bowing: in some, the preferred time is considered; in others, the obligatory recitation is considered, meaning if he has recited the amount that makes the prayer valid, he bows. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. And the intention of prompting someone other than his imam is to teach him with the intent of instruction; as for if he intends to recite the Quran, it does not invalidate according to all. Thus in al-Khulasa and others. And he gave absolute generality in the aforementioned prompting, so it includes whether it is repeated or done once, the most correct, because since it is considered speech, it makes itself cutting, without distinction between little and much, as in al-Jami' al-Saghir. And al-Bada'i' distinguished: if he prompts after opening, his prayer invalidates with one instance; if without opening, it does not invalidate with one instance but does with repetition. This is contrary to the madhhab as you have heard. And it includes whether the one being prompted is praying or not. And the author indicated that if the praying person takes from one who is not prohibited, and...
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling of 'فتح' (prompting) in prayer, which refers to one praying person completing a verse for another. The matn states that prompting someone other than one's imam invalidates the prayer because it constitutes teaching and learning without a necessity. However, prompting the imam is an exception: it does not invalidate because it serves to correct the imam's recitation and thus perfects the prayer. The proof is the hadith of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that the imam's silence is a request for feeding (i.e., prompting). This applies whether the imam has already recited or not, though there is disagreement if the imam has moved to another verse; the correct view is that it still does not invalidate. The authentic Hanafi position from al-Muhit and the works of Imam Muhammad is that prompting the imam is absolutely permissible and does not invalidate the prayer of either the prompter or the imam (if he follows the prompt). However, it is disliked (makruh) for the follower to prompt immediately after opening, and it is also disliked for the imam to deliberately pause to force prompting; instead, the imam should bow when appropriate or move to another verse or surah. For prompting someone other than the imam, the invalidation is contingent on intent: if the prompter intends to teach (ta'lim), it invalidates; if he intends simply to recite Quran (tilawa), it does not, because recitation is part of prayer. The text also notes a dispute: according to al-Bada'i', if the prompt occurs after the imam has opened the recitation, one instance invalidates; otherwise, only repetition does. But the majority (including al-Jami' al-Saghir) hold that any prompting of a non-imam invalidates regardless of frequency, since it is considered speech that cuts the prayer.

Key Terms
فتح (fath): Prompting or completing a verse for someone reciting in prayer; specifically, a praying person saying the next part of a verse for another.
استطعام (istiṭ‘ām): Literally 'seeking food'; in this context, the imam's silence after hesitation indicates a request for prompting.
مقتدي (muqtadī): A follower praying behind an imam (ma'mum).
تعليم وتعلم (ta‘līm wa ta‘allum): Teaching and learning; considered invalidating in prayer because it resembles worldly speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following statements about prompting (fath) in the Hanafi prayer is correct?
A) Prompting the imam always invalidates the prayer.
B) Prompting someone other than the imam invalidates the prayer if done with the intent of teaching.
C) Prompting the imam is permissible but invalidates the prayer if the imam has already recited the verse.
D) Prompting a non-imam is always permissible if the intent is to recite Quran.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, prompting the imam is permissible and does not invalidate anyone's prayer, whereas prompting a non-imam invalidates if done with teaching intent, regardless of frequency.

Ruling on Prompting (Fat-h) for Someone Other than One's Imam
Explaining the ruling and proof for fat-h invalidating prayer when done for a non-imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when prompting (fat-h) during prayer invalidates the prayer according to the Hanafi school, explain the proof from the hadith of Ali (ra), and identify the difference of opinion between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Prompting a non-imam with teaching intent invalidates prayer; prompting one's own imam does not.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author examines the ruling on a person prompting someone other than his imam during prayer. The key distinction is whether the prompting is for one's own imam or for another person, and what intention the prompter has. We will walk through the ruling, its proof, and the internal Hanafi disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Recognize that 'fat-h' means prompting the next part of the Quran. The default ruling is that it invalidates prayer if done for a non-imam with teaching intent.
The passage states: 'And his opening for someone other than his imam i.e., it invalidates it because it is teaching and learning without need.'
2
Distinguish between prompting one's own imam and another person
Note the restriction: prompting one's own imam does not invalidate, because it corrects the prayer. The author provides examples and cites the hadith of Ali (ra) as proof.
The silence of the imam is interpreted as a request for prompting.
3
Understand the proof from the hadith of Ali (ra)
The hadith 'When the imam asks you for food, feed him; his asking is his silence' is used to permit prompting the imam. The metaphorical 'food' is the Quranic verse needed.
This is a qawl sahabi with zanni thubut and dalala.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf holds that prompting never invalidates because it is Quran recitation and intention does not change its nature. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad distinguish based on intent and context.
The relied-upon position follows Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi position
The mufta bihi is that prompting a non-imam with teaching intent invalidates, while prompting one's own imam does not. This is confirmed from Al-Kafi and other sources.
The author states: 'the correct in the madhhab is that opening for one's imam invalidates the prayer of no one'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها
قوله : ( وفتحه على غير إمامه أي يفسدها لأنه تعليم وتعلم لغير حاجة . قيد به لأنه لو فتح على إمامه فلا فساد لأنه تعلق به إصلاح صلاته أما إن كان الإمام لم يقرأ الفرض فظاهر، وأما إن كان قرأ ففيه اختلاف والصحيح عدم الفساد لأنه لو لم يفتح ربما يجري على لسانه ما يكون مفسداً فكان فيه إصلاح صلاته ولاطلاق ما روي عن علي رضي الله عنه إذا استطعمكم الإمام فأطعموه واستطعامه سكوته. ولهذا لو فتح على إمامه بعدما انتقل إلى آية أخرى لا تفسد صلاته وهو قول عامة المشايخ لإطلاق المرخص. وفي المحيط ما يفيد أنه المذهب فإن فيه وذكر في الأصل والجامع الصغير أنه إذا فتح على إمامه يجوز مطلقاً لأن الفتح وإن كان تعليماً ولكن التعليم ليس بعمل كثير وأنه تلاوة حقيقة فلا يكون مفسداً وإن لم يكن محتاجاً إليه وصحح في الظهيرية أنه لا تفسد صلاة الفاتح على كل حال وتفسد صلاة الإمام إذا أخذ من الفاتح بعدما انتقل إلى آية أخرى. وصحح المصنف في الكافي أنه لا تفسد صلاة الإمام أيضاً، فصار الحاصل أن الصحيح من المذهب أن الفتح على إمامه لا صلاة أحد، لا الفاتح ولا الآخذ مطلقاً في كل حال. ثم قيل: ينوي الفاتح بالفتح على إمامه التلاوة، والصحيح أنه نوي الفتح دون القراءة لأن قراءة المقتدي منهي عنها والفتح على إمامه عنه. قالوا : يكره للمقتدي أن يفتح على إمامه من ساعته، وكذا يكره للإمام أن يلجئهم إليه بأن يقف ساكتاً بعد الحصر أو يكرر الآية بل يركع إذا جاء أوانه أو ينتقل إلى آية أخرى لم يلزم من وصلها ما يفسد الصلاة أو ينتقل إلى سورة أخرى كما في المحيط . واختلفت الرواية في وقت أوان الركوع ففي بعضها اعتبر أوانه المستحب، وفي بعضها اعتبر فرض القراءة يعني إذا قرأ مقدار ما تجوز به الصلاة ركع. كذا في السراج الوهاج. وأراد من الفتح على غير إمامه تلقينه على قصد التعليم، أما إن قصد قراءة القرآن فلا تفسد عند الكل. كذا في الخلاصة وغيرها. وأطلق في الفتح المذكور فشمل ما إذا تكرر منه أو كان مرة واحدة الأصح لأنه لما اعتبر كلاماً جعل نفسه قاطعاً. من غير فصل بين القليل والكثير كما في الجامع الصغير وفصل في البدائع بأنه إن فتح بعد استفتاح فصلاته تفسد بمرة واحدة، وإن كان من غير استفتاح فلا تفسد بمرة واحدة وإنما تفسد بالتكرار .اهـ. وهو خلاف المذهب كما سمعت، وشمل ما إذا كان المفتوح عليه مصلياً أو لا . وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو أخذ المصلي غير منهي وهو إلا الله» قال: وما سلكناه أولى قوله : (لأنه تعليم وتعلم لغير حاجة لأن المستفتح كأنه يقول إذا انتهيت إلى هذا فبعده ماذا ، والذي فتح عليه كأنه يقول إذا انتهيت إلى هذا فبعده هذا فيكون من كلام الناس. كذا في السراج قوله : ففي بعضها اعتبر أوانه (المستحب قال في فتح القدير : وهذا هو الظاهر من جهة الدليل ألا ترى إلى ما ذكروا أنه قال لأبي : هلا فتحت علي؟ مع أنها كانت سورة المؤمنين بعد الفاتحة قوله : (وأطلق في الفتح المذكور) أي أطلق المصنف في الفتح المفسد وهو ما غير الإمام بفتح من فتح عليه فإن صلاته تفسد كما في الخلاصة. ثم اعلم أن هذا كله على قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد، وأما على قول أبي يوسف فلا تفسد صلاة الفاتح مطلقاً لأنه قرآن فلا يتغير بقصد القارىء عنده . وفي القنية: ارتج على الإمام ففتح عليه من ليس في صلاته وتذكر فإذا أخذ في التلاوة قبل تمام الفتح لم تفسد وإلا فتفسد لأن تذكره يضاف إلى الفتح، وفتح المراهق كالبالغ. ولو سمعه المؤتم ممن ليس في الصلاة ففتحه على إمامه يجب أن تبطل صلاة الكل لأن التلقين من خارج اهـ .
Book of Prayer, Chapter on What Invalidates Prayer and What is Disliked in It. His statement: 'And his opening for someone other than his imam' i.e., it invalidates it because it is teaching and learning without need. It is restricted because if he opened for his imam, then no invalidation because his prayer's correction is attached to it. If the imam had not recited the obligatory part, it is apparent; but if he had recited, there is disagreement, and the correct view is no invalidation because if he did not open, perhaps something invalidating might come to his tongue, so it corrects his prayer. And due to the absolute meaning of what was narrated from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'When the imam asks you for food, feed him; and his asking is his silence.' Hence, if he opened for his imam after moving to another verse, his prayer is not invalidated, and this is the statement of the general scholars due to the absolute permission. In Al-Muhit, it indicates that it is the madhhab, for it mentions in Al-Asl and Al-Jami' al-Saghir that if he opens for his imam, it is absolutely permissible because the opening, even if it is teaching, but teaching is not a great action, and it is recitation in reality, so it does not invalidate even if there is no need. And it was confirmed in Al-Zahiriyyah that the prayer of the opener is not invalidated in any case, but the prayer of the imam is invalidated if he takes from the opener after moving to another verse. And the author confirmed in Al-Kafi that the prayer of the imam is also not invalidated, so the conclusion is that the correct in the madhhab is that opening for one's imam invalidates the prayer of no one, neither the opener nor the taker, absolutely in every case. Then it is said: The opener intends recitation by opening for his imam, but the correct is that he intends opening, not recitation, because the follower is prohibited from recitation and opening for his imam is permissible. They said: It is disliked for the follower to open for his imam immediately, and likewise it is disliked for the imam to force them to it by remaining silent after being stumped or repeating the verse; rather, he should bow when its time comes or move to another verse or to another surah, as in Al-Muhit. And the narration differs regarding the time of bowing: in some, the recommended time is considered; in others, the obligatory recitation is considered, meaning when he recites the amount that suffices for prayer, he bows. Like that in Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. And what is meant by opening for someone other than his imam is teaching him with the intention of instruction. But if he intends recitation of the Quran, it does not invalidate according to all. Like that in Al-Khulasa and others. And he made absolute in the mentioned opening, so it includes whether it is repeated from him or once—this is the most correct because when it is considered speech, it becomes a disconnecter, without distinction between little and much, as in Al-Jami' al-Saghir. But there is a distinction in Al-Bada'i that if he opened after the opening takbir, his prayer is invalidated by one time, but if not after the opening takbir, it is not invalidated by one time but only by repetition. End quote. And it is contrary to the madhhab as you have heard. And it includes whether the one opened for is praying or not. And the author indicated that if the praying person took other than prohibited... and his statement: 'And what we have followed is more appropriate.' His statement: 'Because it is teaching and learning without need' because the one asking for opening is as if saying, 'When I reach this, what is after it?' and the one opening is as if saying, 'When you reach this, after it is this.' Thus it becomes from people's speech. Like that in Al-Siraj. His statement: 'In some, the recommended time is considered.' He said in Fath al-Qadir: 'And this is apparent from the perspective of evidence; do you not see what they mentioned that he said to Ubayy: Why did you not open for me? even though it was Surah al-Mu'minun after al-Fatihah.' His statement: 'And he made absolute in the mentioned opening.' i.e., the author made absolute in the invalidating opening, which is for other than the imam, by opening of the one who opened upon him, for his prayer is invalidated as in Al-Khulasa. Then know that all this is according to the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. As for the statement of Abu Yusuf, the prayer of the opener is not invalidated at all because it is Quran, so it is not changed by the intention of the reader according to him. And in Al-Qunyah: The imam was stumped, so someone not in prayer opened for him and then remembered; if he took up recitation before the opening was complete, it does not invalidate; otherwise it invalidates because his remembering is attributed to the opening. And the opening of an adolescent is like an adult. And if the follower hears from someone not in prayer and he opens for his imam, it is necessary that the prayer of all becomes invalid because the prompting is from outside. End quote.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the ruling on a follower prompting (fat-h) someone other than his imam during prayer. The author states that such prompting invalidates the prayer because it constitutes teaching and learning without need. The restriction is important: if the prompting is for one's own imam, it does not invalidate because it aims to correct the prayer. Evidence is cited from the narration of Ali (ra): 'When the imam asks you for food, feed him; his asking is his silence.' This indicates that prompting the imam is akin to responding to a request. Several scenarios are discussed: if the imam has already recited the obligatory part, there is disagreement, but the correct view is no invalidation since the prompting prevents worse errors. The text then clarifies that the correct madhhab is that prompting one's own imam does not invalidate the prayer for anyone—neither the prompter nor the imam who follows the prompt. However, prompting a non-imam with the intent of teaching invalidates the prayer; but if one intends simply to recite Quran, it does not invalidate. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that prompting never invalidates because it is Quran recitation and intention does not change its nature, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad distinguish based on intent. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that prompting a non-imam invalidates prayer, in line with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The passage also covers subsidiary issues such as the imam's duty to avoid forcing followers to prompt him, and the case where a person outside prayer prompts the imam.

Key Terms
الفتح (al-fatih): Promting or teaching the imam or another person during prayer by supplying the next part of Quran.
الإمام (al-imam): The leader of the congregational prayer.
تعليم (ta'lim): Teaching or instructing, which if done during prayer without necessity can invalidate it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does opening (fat-h) for someone other than one's imam invalidate the prayer?
A) Always, regardless of intent.
B) Only if done with the intention of teaching (ta'lim).
C) Only if the person being prompted is also in prayer.
D) Never, because it is recitation of Quran.

Key Takeaway
The intent and the recipient of prompting determine whether it invalidates prayer: prompting one's own imam is always valid, while prompting a non-imam invalidates only if done with teaching intent.

Ruling on Answering with 'La ilaha illa Allah' in Prayer: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explaining ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on responding with 'la ilaha illa Allah'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on responding with 'la ilaha illa Allah' during prayer and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Answering with dhikr in prayer invalidates if intended as human speech; majority holds it invalid, Abu Yusuf says it remains praise.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a nuanced issue: what happens if a person in prayer utters 'la ilaha illa Allah' as a response to something said by another? The Hanafi school is divided. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani rule that it invalidates the prayer, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. This lesson breaks down the disagreement, the evidence, and the practical implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the split
Recognize that the act is uttering 'la ilaha illa Allah' in prayer as a reply. State the positions: Abu Hanifa & Muhammad say it invalidates; Abu Yusuf says it does not.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between madhahib.
2
State Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's proof
Their proof: the phrase becomes human speech (kalam al-nas) when intended as a response, analogous to agreed-upon invalidating cases like addressing someone by name using Quranic verses.
See the examples: 'O Yahya take the book', etc.
3
State Abu Yusuf's proof
Abu Yusuf argues that the phrase is inherently praise (thana) and dhikr; intention does not change its essential nature, so it remains valid.
He considers the form of the words, not the context.
4
Examine the supporting examples
The text lists agreed-upon invalidating cases: addressing a person named Yahya, Musa, etc., with Quranic verses. These show that when speech is directed to someone, it becomes human speech.
These examples strengthen the majority view and refute Abu Yusuf.
5
Consider the principle of intention
The passage concludes that in matters of worship, inner intention (ma fi nafs al-amr) governs, not outward appearance. If the person intended response, it invalidates; if pure dhikr, it does not.
This supports the majority view that intention can change the ruling.

جواباً كتشميت العاطس وليس مقصود المصنف خصوص الجواب بهذه الكلمة هي ذكر أو قرآن قصد بها الجواب فهي على الخلاف كما إذا أخبر بخبر يسره فقال الحمد لله، أو بأمر عجيب فقال سبحان الله . ثم نص المشايخ على والإيراد مدفوع من أصله لأن أبا يوسف لا يقول بالفساد بالفتح على غير إمامه كما ذكره الزيلعي يكون على غير إمامه. قوله : (وفي القنية ارتج على الإمام إلى قوله (وتذكر ) أقول : يحتمل أن يكون المراد أنه تذكر بسبب الفتح وأن يكون تذكر بنفسه ولكنه صادف تذكره وفتح من ليس في صلاته في وقت واحد والظاهر الأول، لأنه لو كان تذكره من نفسه لا يظهر فرق بين أخذه في التلاوة قبل تمام الفتح أو بعده، ولا يظهر وجه الفساد Abu Yusuf said: It is not an invalidator because it is praise in its form, so it is not changed by one's intention. According to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), he placed the speech in the context of an answer and it is possible, so it becomes an answer like the blessing of a sneezer. And the author's intention is not specifically the answer with this word; it is remembrance or Quran if intended as answer, so it is subject to the disagreement, as when someone is told good news and says 'Alhamdulillah', or is told something amazing and says 'Subhanallah'. Then the scholars stated things that cause invalidation by agreement: for example, if in front of the praying person there is a book, and there is a man named Yahya, and he says 'O Yahya, take the book with strength' [Maryam:12]; or a man named Musa with a staff in his hand and he says to him 'And what is that in your right hand, O Musa' [Taha:17]; or he is in a ship and his son is outside and he says 'O my son, embark with us' [Hud:42]; or there is a knock on the door or a call from outside and he says 'Whoever enters it is safe' [Aal-e-Imran:97]; and he intended by these words address (khitab), because it is not unclear to anyone that he is speaking, not reciting. And these support what they (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) said and are refutations against Abu Yusuf. And among the objections raised against Abu Yusuf is the opening (fat-h) for other than one's imam, for it is an invalidator according to him and it is Quran. Like that in Fath al-Qadir. And he answered it in Ghayat al-Bayan that the invalidation according to him is for another reason, which is teaching, and the objection is fundamentally invalid because Abu Yusuf does not say that opening for other than one's imam invalidates, as al-Zaylai mentioned it is for other than one's imam. His statement: 'And in Al-Qunyah: The imam was stumped, so someone not in prayer opened for him and then remembered; if he took up recitation before the opening was complete, it does not invalidate; otherwise it invalidates because his remembering is attributed to the opening.' I say: It may be that what is meant is that he remembered due to the opening, and it may be that he remembered by himself but coincidentally his remembering and the opening of the person not in prayer occurred at the same time. The first is apparent, because if he remembered by himself, there would be no difference between his taking recitation before or after the completion of the opening, and the face of invalidation would not be apparent. For invalidation is not merely from the opening; rather it is from taking due to the opening. And if his remembering was from himself, the taking due to the opening would not have occurred. And the fact that the apparent is that he took due to the opening, so it is attributed to it, not what is in the actual matter, because that is from religious matters, not from matters that go back to judging so that the outward is considered. And evidence for that is what has passed: that if he opened for other than his imam intending recitation, not teaching, it does not invalidate according to all; and that if he heard the adhan and said like what the muadhdhin says, it invalidates if he intends answer, otherwise not; and similar matters where what is in one's inner self is considered, not the apparent meaning. This is what appears to me, so let it be pondered. His statement: 'And they support what they said and are refutations against Abu Yusuf.' I say: The apparent is that the invalidation according to Abu Yusuf is not due to change by intention, but rather because it contains what is contrary to it; what is intended as answer and not address. The conclusion is that he distinguished between intending answer and intending address. A lesson for him with the self of, the address.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the ruling on a person in prayer saying 'La ilaha illa Allah' as a response to something. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if the phrase is uttered as a reply (jawab) to a question or statement, it invalidates the prayer because it is considered speech from people, not pure Quranic recitation or dhikr. However, Abu Yusuf argues that it does not invalidate because 'la ilaha illa Allah' is dhikr (remembrance) and its form is praise, and intention does not change its essence. The text then provides examples that are agreed upon as invalidating prayer: if one addresses a person by name using a Quranic verse (e.g., 'O Yahya, take the book'), it is considered speech, not recitation, and invalidates. These examples support the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. A further objection is raised against Abu Yusuf: if opening (fat-h) for a non-imam invalidates according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad even though it is Quran, then by analogy, answering with 'la ilaha illa Allah' should also invalidate. But the text responds that Abu Yusuf himself does not hold that fat-h invalidates, so the objection is baseless. The passage concludes with a discussion of intent: in matters of worship, what matters is the inner intention (ma fi nafs al-amr), not the apparent meaning. Thus, if the person intended answer, it invalidates; if they intended pure dhikr, it does not.

Key Terms
الجواب (al-jawab): An answer or response given to a prior statement or question.
الثناء (al-thana): Praise, specifically as a form of dhikr.
الخطاب (al-khitab): Addressing someone directly, as opposed to mere recitation. This is considered speech and invalidates prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if a person in prayer says 'La ilaha illa Allah' as an answer to a question?
A) It invalidates the prayer according to all Hanafi imams.
B) It does not invalidate because it is pure remembrance.
C) It invalidates according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but not according to Abu Yusuf.
D) It is recommended and increases reward.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, uttering Quranic words as a response in prayer invalidates if intended as human speech, based on the majority of the school; intention is the key factor.

Ruling on Responding with La Ilaha Illa Allah in Prayer
Intra-Hanafi dispute on invalidating prayer by saying 'la ilaha illa Allah' as a response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf on saying 'la ilaha illa Allah' as a response in prayer and identify the thamarah al-khilaf of this intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
Intention turns dhikr into speech: saying 'la ilaha illa Allah' as a response invalidates prayer per Abu Hanifa.

You are in prayer when someone tells you good news. Instinctively, you say 'Alhamdulillah'. Did your prayer remain valid? The answer is not straightforward and reveals a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: the role of intention (niyya) in transforming the nature of an utterance. This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether responding with 'la ilaha illa Allah' (or similar phrases) invalidates prayer, and why the majority holds that it does.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Identify the two positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it invalidates; Abu Yusuf says it does not.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf between the three founding imams.
2
Identify the opponent's proof
For Abu Yusuf, the phrase 'la ilaha illa Allah' is inherently praise (thana') in its wording. Intention does not change its essential form, so it remains dhikr and does not invalidate.
3
Analyse the majority's proof
For Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the person is using the phrase as a response, making it analogous to replying to a sneeze. The utterance becomes human speech (kalam) because of the intention, thus invalidating prayer.
The text provides consensus examples where addressing a person with Quranic verses (e.g., 'Ya Yahya') invalidates prayer, supporting this analogy.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a worshipper responds to an external event with 'la ilaha illa Allah' (or similar), the prayer is invalid according to the majority, but valid according to Abu Yusuf.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is invalidation, as it aligns with consensus examples.
