Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Definition of Excessive Action (Amal Kathir) in Prayer
Discussion of definitions of excessive action (amal kathir) and their applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two main Hanafi definitions of amal kathir, identify the preferred one, and apply it to practical cases to determine if an action invalidates prayer.

Core Idea
An action in prayer invalidates if an observer would be certain you are not praying.

This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi debate on what constitutes 'excessive action' (amal kathir) that nullifies prayer. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents multiple definitions, critiques the second based on two-handedness, and settles on the observer-certainty criterion. Understanding this dispute is crucial for correctly identifying which actions break prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its significance
The overall ruling is that committing 'amal kathir intentionally invalidates prayer (batil). Understand that this is a key concept in Hanafi fiqh with many practical applications.
2
State the first definition (observer certainty)
The first and most authentic definition: any action about which a neutral observer (unaware of the prayer) would have no doubt that the person is not in prayer is 'much'. If the observer hesitates, it is 'little'.
This definition is preferred by the majority of later Hanafi authorities.
3
State the second definition (two-hand criterion) and its conditions
The second definition: actions normally done with two hands are 'much' even if done with one; actions normally done with one hand are 'little' even if done with two. Some add a condition that one-handed actions repeated three times consecutively become 'much'.
This view is attributed to Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl but is critiqued.
4
Analyze the critique against the second definition
The author provides counterexamples: chewing gum invalidates despite using one hand; oiling hair is usually one-handed but invalidates; kissing and touching also invalidate despite not being manual actions. These show the two-hand criterion is inconsistent.
The first definition handles these cases uniformly.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (preferred view) and its application
The relied-upon view is the first definition (observer certainty). Apply it to new cases: ask yourself, 'Would an observer be certain the person is not praying?' If yes, the action is 'much' and invalidates; if the observer would doubt, it is 'little' and does not invalidate.
This definition is pragmatic and aligns with custom ('urf).

إسماعيل بعد ذكر الدرر : هذا القول الثاني وهو اختيار الشيخ الإمام أبي بكر محمد بن الفضل . كذا في الخانية والخلاصة. وقدمه جازماً به في المجموع واقتصر عليه العتابي. وفي عمدة المفتي ثم قال : بل ظاهر ما في الكلام في اه.

كتاب الصلاة باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها
۲۱
الثلاث بيد واحدة لا تفسد، وتعليل الولوالجي بأن تسريح الشعر يفعل باليدين ممنوع. وأما قولهم ولو حملت صبياً فأرضعته تفسد فهو على سائر التفاسير لكن ما في الخلاصة والخانية : المرأة إذا أرضعت ولدها تفسد صلاتها لأنها صارت مرضعة، فشمل ما إذا حمل إليها فدفعت إليه الثدي فرضعها، وأما إذا ارتضع من ثديها وهي كارهة ففي الظهيرية والخلاصة والخانية إن مص ثلاثاً فسدت وإن لم ينزل اللبن فإن كان مصة أو مصتين فإن نزل لبن فسدت وإلا فلا. وفي المنية والمحيط: إن خرج اللبن فسدت وإلا فلا من غير تقييد بعدد. وصححه في معراج الدراية. وأما قولهم لو ضرب إنساناً بيد واحدة أو بسوط تفسد كما في المحيط والخلاصة والظهيرية والمنية فلا يتفرع على ما يقام باليدين على الصحيح لكن في الظهيرية لو ضرب دابته مرة أو مرتين لا تفسد وإن ضربها ثلاثاً في ركعة واحدة تفسد قال رضي الله عنه : وعندي إذا ضرب مرة واحدة وسكن ثم ضرب مرة أخرى وسكن ثم ضرب مرة أخرى لا تفسد صلاته كما قلنا في المشي ا هـ. وهذا يطلح أن يتفرع على القولين، وأما اعتبارهم المرات في الحك كما قدمناه عن الخلاصة فالظاهر تفريعه على قول من فسر العمل الكثير بما تكرر ثلاثاً وهو القول الثالث لا على القولين الأولين، وأما قولهم لو قتل القملة مراراً إن قتل قتلاً متداركاً تفسد، وإن كان بین القتلات فرجة لا تفسد فيصلح تفريعه على الأقوال كلها. وإما قولهم لو قبل المصلي امرأته بشهوة أو بغير شهوة أو مسها بشهوة فسدت ينبغي تفريعه على القول الأصح، وكذا على قول من فسر العمل الكثير بما يستفحشه المصلي. وأما على اعتبار ما يفعل باليدين أو بما تكرر ثلاثاً فلا وهو مما يضعفهما كما لا يخفي. كذا لو جامعها فيما دون الفرج من غير إنزال بخلاف النظر إلى فرجها بشهوة فإنه يفسد على المختار كما الخلاصة. وأما قولهم كما في الخانية والخلاصة لو كانت المرأة هي المصلية دونه فقبلها فسدت بشهوة أو بغير شهوة ولو كان هو المصلي فقبلته ولم يشتهها فصلاته تامة فمشكل، إذ ليس من المصلي فعل في الصورتين فمقتضاه عدم الفساد فيهما، فإن جعلنا تمكينه من الفعل بمنزلة فعله اقتضى الفساد فيهما وهو الظاهر على اعتبار أن العمل الكثير ما لو نظر إليه الناظر لتيقن أنه ليس في الصلاة أو ما استفحشه المصلي لكن في شرح الزاهدي : ولو قبل المصلية لا في التسريح والجواب تعليل صاحب الهداية له بقوله في التجنيس لأنه يقوم باليدين غالباً قوله : (وأما إذا ارتضع من ثديها كذا في بعض النسخ بـ«أما الشرطية، وفي بعضها وما إذا بدون همزة وعليها يتوجه قول النهر هذا سهو ظاهر وأنى يقال ارتضاعه من غير فعل منها أنها أرضعته ا هـ . ويؤيد النسخة الأولى أن المعنى عليها وذكر الفاء في جواب «أما قوله : (وأما قولهم كما في الخانية والخلاصة إلى قوله فمشكل) قال في الفتح بعد نقله ذلك عن الخلاصة : والله تعالى أعلم بوجه الفرق. وفي النهر : وعلى ما في الخلاصة قد فرق بأن الشهوة لما كانت في النساء أغلب كان تقبيله مستلزماً لاشتهائها عادة بخلاف تقبيلها اهـ. ومثله في شرح العلامة المقدسي بزيادة وعبارته وفتح الله سبحانه وتعالى
The Book of Prayer, Chapter on What Invalidates Prayer and What is Disliked in It. Then they differed on what determines much and little into several sayings: One is what the public chose as in al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya: every action that an observer has no doubt that it is not in prayer is much, and every action that an observer hesitates whether its doer is in prayer is little. Al-Bada'i' said this is the most correct. The commentator and al-Walwaliji followed him. Al-Muhit said it is the best. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said it is the truth. Al-Halabi mentioned that apparently by 'observer' they mean someone not aware of the prayer's start; then if he sees this action and is certain it is not in prayer, it is much action, and if he doubts, it is little. Second: that what is normally done with two hands is much even if done with one hand, like wearing a turban, putting on a shirt, tying trousers, and shooting a bow; and what is done with one hand is little even if done with two hands, like removing a shirt, untying trousers, putting on a cap, removing it, removing a bridle, and the like. The commentator mentioned it thus, but al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya did not restrict what is done with two hands by custom, and al-Khaniyya restricted what is done with one hand to when not repeated, and by repetition they mean three consecutive times, as in al-Khulasa: if he scratched three times in one pillar his prayer is invalidated. This is if he lifts his hand each time; if he does not lift each time, it does not invalidate because it is one scratch. This is a strange restriction and a wonderful detail that should be memorized. But in al-Zahiriyya attributed to al-Sadr al-Shahid Hisam al-Din: if he scratched a spot on his body three times at once, his prayer is invalidated. And I have not seen anyone who has authenticated the second saying on defining action, and it may be said that it is not sound, and if he chewed gum in his prayer, his prayer is invalidated—Muhammad mentioned it as in al-Bada'i', because the observer from afar has no doubt that he is not in prayer, and it involves no use of the hand at all, let alone the use of two hands. Likewise, eating and drinking are done with one hand and invalidate by consensus. And likewise their saying: if he oiled his head or combed his hair, whether the hair of his head or his beard, his prayer is invalidated, it does not derive from the view that much action is that done with two hands, because oiling the head and combing hair are usually done with one hand, unless by 'oiling' he means taking the bottle and pouring the oil from it with his other hand, and that is so, for in al-Muhit it said: and if he poured oil on his head, because it is mostly from issues of previous hadath (ritual impurity) which does not negate the lack of invalidation. His statement: 'and I have not seen anyone who authenticated the second saying'—Shaykh Isma'il said after mentioning al-Durar: this second saying is the choice of the eminent Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl. So it is in al-Khaniyya and al-Khulasa, and he presented it decisively in al-Majmu' and al-Utabi limited to it, and in Umdat al-Mufti then he said: rather the apparent from al-Hawi at the end of the subdivision on it is his statement: 'and it may be said that it is not sound etc.' He said in al-Nahr: it is not hidden that the restriction of 'whereby' is observed; so the meaning of 'what is done with two hands is much' means from the aspect that it is done with them, but on this, the ruling on chewing gum remains unknown, and there is no obstacle to considering something else in this saying that includes it. His statement: 'if he chewed gum in his prayer, it is invalidated etc.' means if the chewing is much, as in al-Tajnis. His statement: 'it is done with one hand' will come. His statement: 'unless by oiling he means taking it etc.' and the discussion remains. ... the three with one hand does not invalidate, and al-Walwaliji's reasoning that combing hair is done with two hands is rejected. As to their saying: if a woman carried a child and breastfed him, it invalidates, it is upon all interpretations, but in al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya: if a woman breastfeeds her child, her prayer is invalidated because she becomes a breastfeeding woman; this includes if the child was brought to her and she gave him the breast and he suckled. But if he suckled from her breast while she was unwilling, in al-Zahiriyya, al-Khulasa, and al-Khaniyya: if he sucked three times, it invalidates even if milk did not come out; if one or two sucks, then if milk came out, it invalidates, otherwise not. In al-Munya and al-Muhit: if milk came out, it invalidates, otherwise not, without restriction by number. And it was authenticated in Mi'raj al-Diraya. And as to their saying: if he struck a person with one hand or a whip, it invalidates, as in al-Muhit, al-Khulasa, al-Zahiriyya, and al-Munya, it does not derive from 'what is done with two hands' according to the correct view. But in al-Zahiriyya: if he struck his beast once or twice, it does not invalidate; if he struck it three times in one rak'ah, it invalidates. He (رضي الله عنه) said: According to me, if he struck once and then stopped, then struck again and stopped, then struck again, his prayer is not invalidated as we said in walking. This seems to derive from the two sayings. And as to their consideration of repetitions in scratching as we presented from al-Khulasa, apparently it derives from the saying of one who defines much action as that which is repeated three times, which is the third saying, not the first two. And as to their saying: if he killed lice repeatedly, if he killed with continuous killing it invalidates, and if there was an interval between killings it does not invalidate, this can derive from all sayings. And as to their saying: if a praying person kisses his wife with desire or without, or touches her with desire, it invalidates, it should derive from the most correct saying, and likewise from the saying of one who defines much action as what the one praying finds repulsive. But according to the consideration of what is done with two hands or what is repeated three times, it does not, and this is something that weakens them, as is not hidden. Likewise, if he had intercourse with her in the private parts without ejaculation, as opposed to looking at her private parts with desire, which invalidates according to the chosen view, as in al-Khulasa. And as to their saying, as in al-Khaniyya and al-Khulasa: if the woman was the one praying and he kissed her, it invalidates with or without desire; and if he was the one praying and she kissed him and he did not desire it, his prayer is complete—this is problematic, since there is no action from the one praying in both cases, so the requirement is no invalidation in both. If we consider his enabling the action as his own action, it would require invalidation in both, and that is apparent according to the consideration that much action is what, if the observer looks at it, he would be certain that he is not in prayer, or what the one praying finds repulsive. But in Sharh al-Zahidi: 'if the woman praying kisses? no' regarding combing, and the answer is the reasoning of the author of al-Hidaya with his statement in al-Tajnis: 'because it is usually done with two hands'. His statement: 'and if he suckles from her breast' is in some copies with the conditional 'amma', and in others 'wa ma idha' without the hamza, and accordingly the statement of al-Nahr 'this is an apparent oversight' is directed, and how can it be said that his suckling without her action means she breastfed him? The first copy is supported because the meaning is what it gives, and the mention of 'fa' in the answer of 'amma'. His statement: 'and as to their statement as in al-Khaniyya and al-Khulasa up to his saying 'problematic'—al-Fath said after quoting it from al-Khulasa: and Allah the Exalted knows best the aspect of the difference. In al-Nahr: and according to al-Khulasa, the differentiation is made on the basis that since desire is more dominant in women, his kissing entails her desire habitually, unlike her kissing him. And similarly in Sharh al-Allamah al-Maqdisi with a further addition: and may Allah the Exalted open.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqaiq's chapter on what invalidates prayer. The author, Ibn Nujaym, discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute on defining 'amal kathir (excessive action) that nullifies salah. The passage begins by stating that scholars differ on the criterion for distinguishing much from little action. The first and most authentic opinion, preferred by the general body and cited in al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya, is that any action about which an observer (who does not know the person started prayer) would have no doubt that it is not part of prayer is considered 'much' and thus invalidates. Conversely, if the observer hesitates, the action is 'little' and does not invalidate. This definition is based on customary perception. The second opinion defines 'much' as what is normally accomplished with two hands, even if done with one, and 'little' as what is normally done with one hand, even if done with two. The author notes that al-Khaniyya adds a condition that for one-handed actions to be 'little' they must not be repeated, with repetition defined as three consecutive times. He calls this restriction 'strange' and 'wonderful'. He then critiques the second opinion, stating that some actions like chewing gum invalidate even though done with one hand, undermining the two-hand criterion. The passage proceeds to examine various cases: applying oil, combing hair, breastfeeding, striking a person or animal, killing lice, kissing, etc., showing how each ruling relates to the definitions. The author points out inconsistencies and emphasizes that the preferred view is the first definition based on observer certainty. The discussion reflects the Hanafi method of testing legal principles through practical examples (tafri') and weighing conflicting opinions to arrive at the mu'tamad (relied-upon) position.

Key Terms
العمل الكثير (al-amal al-kathir): An action during prayer that is considered excessive, leading to invalidation of the prayer if done intentionally.
العمل القليل (al-amal al-qalil): An action during prayer that is considered minor and does not invalidate the prayer.
الناظر (al-nazir): A hypothetical observer who is unaware that the person has begun prayer; used as a criterion for determining whether an action is 'much'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most authentic opinion in this passage, which criterion defines an action as 'much' (الكثير) and thus invalidating prayer?
A) The action is normally done with two hands.
B) An observer seeing the action would have no doubt that the person is not in prayer.
C) The action is repeated three times consecutively.
D) The action involves a lot of movement.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on the observer's certainty to define excessive action in prayer, not on the number of hands used or repetitions.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
Striking During Prayer: How Many Invalidate?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on invalidation by striking; resolution via consecutive strikes criterion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on striking during prayer, distinguish between consecutive and separated strikes, and apply the criterion of 'amal kathir (excessive action) to determine invalidation.

Core Idea
Three consecutive strikes in one rak‘ah invalidate prayer; separated strikes do not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq examines a nuanced issue: does striking a person or animal during salah invalidate the prayer? Earlier texts suggest that any striking invalidates, but the author refines this by applying the general principle of 'amal kathir (excessive action). By analyzing the number and consecutiveness of strikes, we see how Hanafi jurists define 'excessive' and how this aligns with the school’s broader methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Striking during prayer is an extraneous action. Earlier fatwa collections (al-Muhit, al-Khulasa) state that striking with one hand or a whip invalidates prayer.
This is the starting point; the author will refine it.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Zahiriyya distinguishes between striking an animal once or twice (valid) and three times in one rak‘ah (invalid). This shows a disagreement on the threshold.
The ikhtilaf is on the number of strikes that constitute excessive action.
3
Analyze the author's position
The author (radi allahu anhu) argues that if strikes are separated by pauses, they do not accumulate into a single excessive action, analogous to walking (where few steps with pauses are valid).
The key is consecutiveness, not just number.
4
Relate to the definition of 'amal kathir
Excessive action (amal kathir) is defined by what is considered excessive in custom; three consecutive strikes are deemed excessive, while separated strikes are not.
This reflects the third view (counting by repetition).
5
Conclude the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon position: one or two non-consecutive strikes do not invalidate; three consecutive strikes in one rak‘ah invalidate the prayer.
This resolves the internal dispute.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم لو ضرب إنساناً بيد واحدة أو بسوط تفسد كما في المحيط والخلاصة والظهيرية والمنية فلا يتفرع على ما يقام باليدين على الصحيح لكن في الظهيرية لو ضرب دابته مرة أو مرتين لا تفسد وإن ضربها ثلاثاً في ركعة واحدة تفسد قال رضي الله عنه : وعندي إذا ضرب مرة واحدة وسكن ثم ضرب مرة أخرى وسكن ثم ضرب مرة أخرى لا تفسد صلاته كما قلنا في المشي ا هـ. وهذا يطلح أن يتفرع على القولين، وأما اعتبارهم المرات في الحك كما قدمناه عن الخلاصة فالظاهر تفريعه على قول من فسر العمل الكثير بما تكرر ثلاثاً وهو القول الثالث لا على القولين الأولين
As for their statement: 'If he struck a person with one hand or a whip, it invalidates' as in al-Muhit, al-Khulasa, al-Zahiriyya, and al-Munya, it does not branch upon what is performed with two hands according to the correct view. But in al-Zahiriyya: if he struck his animal once or twice, it does not invalidate; if he struck it three times in one rak'ah, it invalidates. He said (may Allah be pleased with him): In my view, if he struck once and paused, then struck again and paused, then struck again, his prayer is not invalidated, as we said about walking. End quote. This is likely to branch on the two views. As for their counting of times in scratching as we previously mentioned from al-Khulasa, the apparent branching is upon the one who interpreted excessive action as that which is repeated three times, which is the third view, not the first two views.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of striking during prayer, whether a person or animal. The general ruling from earlier texts is that striking with one hand or a whip invalidates prayer. However, the author (possibly the commentator) notes that this does not follow from the criterion of 'what is done with two hands' (which is the correct view for excessive action). In al-Zahiriyya, a distinction is made: striking an animal once or twice does not invalidate, but three times in one rak'ah does. The author adds that if the strikes are separated by pauses, they do not count as consecutive and thus do not invalidate, analogous to walking. This shows that the counting of strikes is based on the definition of kathir (excessive action). The ikhtilaf is whether kathir is defined by the commonness of the action, by being done with both hands, or by repetition three times. Here, the view that three consecutive strikes are excessive is considered the third view.

Key Terms
ضرب (darb): Striking a person or animal during salah; a potential invalidator if excessive.
عمل كثير (amal kathir): Excessive action in prayer that invalidates it; defined by convention or repetition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In al-Zahiriyya, what is the ruling if someone strikes an animal three times in one rak'ah?
A) Prayer is valid
B) Prayer is invalid
C) Prayer is valid if no blood
D) Prayer is invalid only if done with two hands

Key Takeaway
Three consecutive strikes in one rak'ah invalidate prayer; fewer or separated strikes do not, based on the Hanafi definition of excessive action.

Killing Lice Continuously During Prayer Invalidates It
Ruling on killing lice repeatedly during prayer with criterion of continuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing lice during prayer and apply the criterion of continuity to determine whether the prayer is invalidated.

Core Idea
Killing lice continuously during prayer invalidates it; pausing between kills keeps the prayer valid.

In Hanafi fiqh, excessive movement (kathīr al-‘amal) during prayer can invalidate it. This ruling clarifies that killing lice repeatedly is judged by continuity: if the killings are unbroken, it is excessive and invalidates; if there is a gap, it is not excessive and the prayer remains valid. This distinction allows the same principle to be applied across different definitions of excessive action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is killing a louse repeatedly during prayer. The ruling is that the prayer is invalidated if the killing is continuous, but not if interrupted.
2
Understand the criterion: continuity vs. interruption
The text uses the term 'qatl mutadārik' (continuous killing) to mean a sequence without pause. A gap (farjah) between killings breaks the continuity and prevents invalidation.
The word 'mutadārik' implies successive actions without delay.
3
Apply the criterion to the concept of excessive action
This ruling is a specific instance of the general principle that excessive action (kathīr al-‘amal) invalidates prayer. Continuity is the measure: if the actions are too close together, they count as one excessive act.
Other definitions of kathīr al-‘amal (e.g., using both hands, or what custom deems excessive) are also accommodated by this ruling.
4
Note the practical application
If you must kill a louse during prayer, do so with a pause between actions (e.g., kill, then lower your hand, then kill again) to avoid invalidation.
This also applies to swatting mosquitoes or similar insects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم لو قتل القملة مراراً إن قتل قتلاً متداركاً تفسد، وإن كان بین القتلات فرجة لا تفسد فيصلح تفريعه على الأقوال كلها.
As for their statement: 'If he killed a louse repeatedly, if he killed it in a continuous manner, it invalidates; if there was a gap between the killings, it does not invalidate' — this can be branched upon all the views.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This is a ruling statement about killing lice during prayer. The criterion is continuity: if the killing actions are consecutive and uninterrupted, it is considered excessive action and invalidates the prayer. If there is a pause (gap) between the killings, it is not considered excessive and does not invalidate. The author notes that this ruling can be applied consistently with all the different opinions about what constitutes excessive action (kathir al-‘amal), whether defined by repetition, by the use of both hands, or by what is considered excessive in custom.

Key Terms
قتل متدارك (qatl mutadārik): Continuous killing without interruption, considered excessive action.
فرجة (farjah): A gap or pause between actions, which breaks continuity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Killing a louse during prayer invalidates if:
A) It is killed once
B) It is killed repeatedly with gaps
C) It is killed continuously without pause
D) It is killed with a weapon

Key Takeaway
The validity of prayer when killing lice repeatedly depends solely on whether the killings are continuous or separated by a pause.

Kissing and Touching in Prayer: Ruling and the Definition of Excessive Action
Ruling on active kissing and touching in prayer, linked to definitions of excessive action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kissing/touching one's spouse in prayer and explain how it depends on the definition of kathir al-‘amal (excessive action).

Core Idea
Kissing or touching with desire invalidates prayer, but the ruling hinges on what counts as excessive action.

The passage opens with a broad statement that kissing (with or without desire) or touching with desire invalidates prayer. However, this ruling is not absolute; it depends on how one defines excessive action (kathir al-‘amal). In this lesson, we unpack the Hanafi definitions and their implications for the ruling on physical contact during salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling
Find the statement 'If the one praying kissed his wife with desire or without desire, or touched her with desire, it invalidates.' Note that this is the common ruling found in later Hanafi sources.
The phrase 'kissed ... with desire or without desire' is striking – it suggests any kiss might be considered excessive.
2
Understand the Connection to Kathir al-‘Amal
Realize that the ruling is 'branched upon the soundest view' and depends on the interpretation of excessive action. The commentator links it to the definition that kathir al-‘amal is what the person praying finds objectionable (istaftahahu).
Alternative definitions (two hands or three repetitions) would not invalidate, as they are less.
3
Compare the Definitions of Kathir
Contrast three definitions: (1) what is done with two hands, (2) what is repeated three times, (3) what an observer would doubt that the person is in prayer. The passage notes that definitions (1) and (2) are weak.
The third definition is preferred by many later Hanafis and leads to invalidation even for a single kiss if it appears excessive.
4
Apply the Definition to Touching and Other Acts
Note that touching with desire similarly invalidates. Also, the passage mentions that looking at the wife's private part with desire invalidates (according to the chosen view). This shows the principle applies to various intimate acts.
Intercourse below the private part without ejaculation also invalidates – a more severe act.
5
Recognize the Intra-Hanafi Nuance
Even within the Hanafi school, there is dispute when the wife initiates the kiss – this is a separate layer that will be explored in the next lesson. Here, focus on the active kiss by the praying person.
The passive case requires different analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما قولهم لو قبل المصلي امرأته بشهوة أو بغير شهوة أو مسها بشهوة فسدت ينبغي تفريعه على القول الأصح، وكذا على قول من فسر العمل الكثير بما يستفحشه المصلي. وأما على اعتبار ما يفعل باليدين أو بما تكرر ثلاثاً فلا وهو مما يضعفهما كما لا يخفي. كذا لو جامعها فيما دون الفرج من غير إنزال بخلاف النظر إلى فرجها بشهوة فإنه يفسد على المختار كما الخلاصة. وأما قولهم كما في الخانية والخلاصة لو كانت المرأة هي المصلية دونه فقبلها فسدت بشهوة أو بغير شهوة ولو كان هو المصلي فقبلته ولم يشتهها فصلاته تامة فمشكل، إذ ليس من المصلي فعل في الصورتين فمقتضاه عدم الفساد فيهما، فإن جعلنا تمكينه من الفعل بمنزلة فعله اقتضى الفساد فيهما وهو الظاهر على اعتبار أن العمل الكثير ما لو نظر إليه الناظر لتيقن أنه ليس في الصلاة أو ما استفحشه المصلي لكن في شرح الزاهدي : ولو قبل المصلية لا تفسد صلاتها. وقال أبو جعفر إن كان بشهوة فسدت ا هـ. وهو مخالف لما في الخلاصة والخانية مسو لتقبيله وتقبيلها.
As for their statement: 'If the one praying kissed his wife with desire or without desire, or touched her with desire, it invalidates' — it should be branched upon the soundest view, and also upon the one who interprets excessive action as that which the one praying finds objectionable. As for the consideration of what is done with two hands or what is repeated three times, it does not, and this weakens them, as is not hidden. Similarly, if he had intercourse with her below the private part without ejaculation — contrary to looking at her private part with desire, for it invalidates according to the chosen view as in al-Khulasa. As for their statement as in al-Khaniyya and al-Khulasa: 'If the woman was the one praying and he kissed her, it invalidates, with desire or without desire; but if he was the one praying and she kissed him and he did not desire her, his prayer is complete' — this is problematic, because there is no act from the one praying in both scenarios, so they would require non-invalidation in both. If we consider his enabling the act as his own act, it would require invalidation in both, which is apparent according to the view that excessive action is what, if looked at by an observer, would be certain that he is not in prayer, or what the one praying finds objectionable. However, in Sharh al-Zahidi: 'If she kissed the male praying person, her prayer does not invalidate.' And Abu Ja'far said: 'If it was with desire, it invalidates.' End quote. This contradicts what is in al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya equating his kissing and her kissing.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on kissing and touching one's spouse during prayer. The general statement that kissing (with or without desire) or touching with desire invalidates is connected to the interpretation of 'excessive action' (kathir al-‘amal) as that which the person praying deems gross. It does not follow the definitions of 'done with two hands' or 'repeated three times'. Then the text enters a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: if the wife kisses her husband while he is praying and he remains passive, is his prayer invalid? Some sources say it invalidates regardless of his desire, while others say it invalidates only if he desires it. The commentator notes the problem: if the passive recipient is not acting, his prayer should remain valid. However, if his enabling her kiss is considered an act, then it would invalidate. The chosen view (mufta bihi) leans towards invalidation because to an observer, the appearance would be that of an excessive action. The passage ends by noting a contradiction in earlier sources.

Key Terms
تقبيل (taqbil): Kissing one's spouse during prayer; may invalidate depending on desire and whether it is considered excessive action.
شهوة (shahwa): Sexual desire; a factor in determining invalidation of prayer when kissing or touching.
عمل كثير (‘amal kathīr): Excessive action; one of the invalidators of prayer if done intentionally. Defined variously: what is done with two hands, what is repeated three times, or what an observer would doubt is part of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the definition of excessive action (kathir al-‘amal) that the commentator relies upon for invalidating kissing?
A) What is done with two hands
B) What is repeated three times
C) What the person praying finds objectionable or what an observer would doubt
D) Any deliberate movement

Key Takeaway
Kissing or touching with desire invalidates prayer, but even without desire, it may invalidate if considered excessive action under the preferred definition.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute: When the Wife Kisses Her Praying Husband
Intra-Hanafi dispute on invalidation when wife kisses praying husband.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conflicting opinions among Hanafi jurists regarding a man's prayer being invalidated by his wife's kiss and evaluate the preferred view.

Core Idea
If a man is kissed by his wife while praying, does his prayer break? The answer differs among Hanafis based on desire, enabling, and the definition of excessive action.

The text presents a specific scenario that troubled later Hanafi jurists: a woman kisses her praying husband. The commentators note contradictory rulings from earlier sources. This lesson traces the intra-Hanafi dispute, the reasoning behind each position, and the attempt to resolve it through the definition of kathir al-‘amal.

Guided Path
1
State the Contradictory Reports
Identify the two main conflicting views: (1) from al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya: if the husband is kissed and does not desire, his prayer is valid; if the wife is kissed, her prayer invalidates regardless. (2) from Sharh al-Zahidi and Abu Ja'far: the wife's prayer does not invalidate, or invalidates only with desire.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; both views are attributed to Hanafi authorities.
2
Analyse the Reasoning Behind Each View
The view that the husband's prayer is valid (if no desire) is based on the principle that he performed no action. The view that it invalidates is based on considering his enabling (tamkin) as equivalent to action, especially when the kiss appears excessive to an observer.
The commentator notes that the sources equating his and her kissing create inconsistency.
3
Connect to the Definition of Kathir al-‘Amal
The commentator suggests that if we adopt the definition of kathir as what an observer would doubt, then even the passive case invalidates because the overall scene (a kiss) looks like an excessive action. This definition removes the contradiction.
This is a resolve offered, but the text acknowledges it is not universally accepted.
4
Identify the Mufta Bihi (Relled-Upon View)
The commentator seems to lean towards invalidation in both active and passive cases based on the observer definition. However, the text does not give a definitive tarjih, indicating ongoing ikhtilaf.
In practice, many later Hanafis consider any intimate contact with desire as invalidating, but the passive case remains problematic.
5
Note the Practical Impact (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
If a man is kissed and does not desire, some Hanafi muftis would rule his prayer valid, while others would require him to repeat it. The difference matters for the person's certainty and for subsequent acts.
This shows how legal theory affects daily worship.

Walking During Prayer: Ruling and Conditions
Ruling on walking while facing qibla during salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions under which walking during prayer does not invalidate and identify actions that do invalidate.

Core Idea
Walking facing qibla without leaving the mosque does not invalidate; turning back or excessive walking does.

Many assume any movement invalidates prayer, but the Hanafi school permits limited walking under specific conditions. This passage from Munyat al-Musalli clarifies the precise boundaries, distinguishing between valid and invalid movement within salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act under discussion is walking (mashy) during prayer. The default ruling is that it does not invalidate if certain conditions are met.
This is a dispensation (rukhsah) for necessity, not an intrinsic part of prayer.
2
List the three conditions for validity
From Munyat al-Musalli: (1) facing the qibla, (2) non-consecutive steps, (3) not leaving the mosque (or rows in open space).
The condition of non-consecutiveness prevents prolonged movement.
3
Identify invalidators
Turning the back to the qibla invalidates. Additionally, according to al-Zahiriyya, excessive walking (kathir) also invalidates.
Excessive walking is determined by custom (urf) or by breaking the continuity of prayer.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a person who takes three steps forward to adjust their position, facing qibla, without leaving the mosque. Their prayer remains valid.
If they turn around to pick up a phone, it invalidates because they turned their back to qibla.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي منية المصلي : المشي في الصلاة إذا كان مستقبل القبلة لا يفسد إذا لم يكن متلاحقاً ولم يخرج من المسجد وفي الفضاء ما لم يخرج عن الصفوف هذا كله إذا لم يستدبر القبلة، وأما إذا استدبرها فسدت وفي الظهيرية : المختار في المشي أنه إذا كثر أفسدها.
And in Munyat al-Musalli: Walking during prayer if he is facing the qibla does not invalidate if it is not consecutive and he does not leave the mosque, and in an open space as long as he does not leave the rows. All this is if he does not turn his back to the qibla. If he turns his back to the qibla, it invalidates. And in al-Zahiriyya: The chosen view regarding walking is that if it is excessive, it invalidates.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on walking during prayer. The key conditions for validity are: (1) the person remains facing the qibla; (2) the walking is not consecutive; (3) within the mosque or in an open space within the rows. If these conditions are met, walking does not invalidate. However, turning the back on the qibla invalidates regardless. Additionally, al-Zahiriyya states that if the walking is excessive (kathir), it invalidates, which aligns with the general principle that excessive action invalidates prayer. The ruling differentiates between mosque and open space: in a mosque, leaving it is a problem; in open space, leaving the rows is problematic.

Key Terms
المشي (al-mashy): Walking during prayer; a minor movement that may or may not invalidate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions invalidates the prayer?
A) Walking a few steps facing the qibla without leaving the mosque
B) Turning the back to the qibla while walking
C) Walking non-consecutive steps in an open space within the rows
D) Walking moderately while remaining in the mosque

Key Takeaway
Walking during prayer does not invalidate if you remain facing qibla, steps are not consecutive, and you stay within the mosque or rows.

Conditions for Walking During Prayer
Ruling on walking in prayer with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions under which walking during prayer does not invalidate it according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Walking invalidates only if consecutive, leaves mosque, or turns from qibla.

In Hanafi fiqh, walking during prayer is not automatically invalidating. The classical scholars set clear boundaries: as long as the worshipper faces the qibla, does not take consecutive steps, and remains within the prayer space, the prayer remains valid. This lesson unpacks these conditions from the text of al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Walking (al-mashy) during prayer is permissible under certain conditions; otherwise it invalidates.
Default assumption is that any unnecessary movement is problematic, but Hanafis allow little movement.
2
List the conditions for validity
Three conditions from Minyat al-Musalli: (1) facing the qibla, (2) steps not consecutive (mutalāḥiq), (3) not leaving the mosque or defined prayer area.
Consecutive steps are those taken without interruption; a single step is usually fine.
3
Understand the violation consequence
Violating any of these conditions—especially turning from qibla—invalidates the prayer.
The text explicitly says if one turns from qibla, the prayer is invalidated (fasid).
4
Note the chosen position on quantity
The relied-upon (al-mukhtār) position is that much (kathīr) walking invalidates, implying little non-consecutive walking is permissible.
This is from al-Zahiriyyah; it settles the intra-madhhab nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي منية المصلي : المشي في الصلاة إذا كان مستقبل القبلة لا يفسد إذا لم يكن متلاحقاً ولم يخرج من المسجد وفي الفضاء ما لم يخرج عن الصفوف هذا كله إذا لم يستدبر القبلة، وأما إذا استدبرها فسدت وفي الظهيرية : المختار في المشي أنه إذا كثر أفسدها. قوله : (وفي الفضاء ما لم يخرج عن الصفوف أقول قال في التجنيس : رجل صلى في الصحراء فتأخر عن موضع قيامه، المختار أنه لا تفسد صلاته ويعتبر مقدار سجوده من خلفه وعن يمينه وعن يساره كما في وجه القبلة سواء، فما لم يتأخر عن هذا الموضع لم يتأخر عن المسجد فلا تفسد صلاته، ولو خط حوله خطاً ولم الخط لكن تأخر عما ذكرنا من الموضع فسدت لأن الخط ليس بشيء ا هـ .
And in Minyat al-Musalli: Walking in prayer, if facing the qibla, does not invalidate if not consecutive and not leaving the mosque, and in open space as long as not leaving the rows. All this if not turning away from the qibla. But if he turns away from it, it invalidates. And in al-Zahiriyyah: The chosen position on walking is that if it is much, it invalidates. His statement: (And in open space as long as not leaving the rows), I say: al-Tajnīs said: A man prayed in the desert and moved back from his standing place; the chosen position is that his prayer is not invalidated, and consideration is given to the extent of his prostration behind him, to his right, and to his left, just like the direction of the qibla. So as long as he does not move beyond this place, he has not exited the mosque, so his prayer is not invalidated. And if he draws a line around him, etc., but moves beyond the mentioned place, it invalidates because the line is nothing.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on walking during prayer. It presents conditions under which walking does not invalidate: facing qibla, not consecutive steps, and not leaving the mosque (or defined prayer area in open space). If these conditions are violated, especially turning away from qibla, prayer becomes invalid. The commentary expands the matn by defining 'open space' limits via al-Tajnīs: the prayer area is the space of one's prostration in all directions, and moving beyond that invalidates. Drawing a line has no legal effect.

Key Terms
المشي (al-mashy): Walking; moving by foot during prayer.
متلاحقاً (mutalāḥiq): Consecutive steps without interruption.
الصفوف (al-ṣufūf): Rows; in open space, the limit of the prayer area defined by prostration distance.
المختار (al-mukhtār): The chosen or relied-upon position in the madhhab for fatwa.
كثير (kathīr): Much; excessive amount of walking that invalidates prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following conditions must be met for walking during prayer NOT to invalidate?
A) The steps must be consecutive.
B) The worshipper must face the qibla.
C) The worshipper must leave the mosque.
D) The walking must be much.

Key Takeaway
Walking during prayer is not inherently invalid; it depends on facing qibla, non-consecutive steps, and staying within the prayer area.

Defining the Prayer Area in Open Space
Definition of prayer area in open space

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the prayer area for a person praying in open space according to the Hanafi school, using the prostration-distance rule.

Core Idea
In open space, the prayer area is the distance of one's prostration in all directions.

The matn states that walking in open space does not invalidate as long as one does not leave 'the rows.' But what constitutes the boundary in a desert without walls? The commentary, quoting al-Tajnīs, provides a practical criterion: the prayer area is marked by the extent of one's prostration behind, to the right, and to the left, similar to the direction of the qibla. This lesson explores that definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The matn uses 'rows' (ṣufūf) for open space; rows are clear in a mosque but need definition in an open field.
The commentator expands the matn to resolve this ambiguity.
2
Understand the analogy
The open space is treated as a mosque; the prayer area is the space equivalent to one's prostrate distance in all four cardinal directions.
Prostration distance means the length from head to knees when prostrating, approximately an arm's length.
3
Apply the rule
If the worshipper moves beyond that prostrate-distance boundary, he has exited the 'mosque' and the prayer invalidates.
Drawing a line around oneself has no legal effect; only actual movement beyond the boundary matters.

Writing During Prayer: When Does It Invalidate?
Hanafi ruling on writing in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on writing during prayer and identify the condition that determines invalidity.

Core Idea
One clear word written visibly invalidates prayer.

Prayer requires concentration and avoidance of extraneous actions. Among actions that can nullify prayer is writing. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on how much writing causes invalidity, addressing a common misconception that only three words matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is writing (kitābah) during prayer. Determine the ruling: it invalidates the prayer if it meets certain conditions.
2
Understand the surface condition
Writing must be on a visible surface (e.g., ground, paper). Writing on something that does not show the script (e.g., moving a finger in the air) is not considered writing and does not invalidate.
Key term: mustabīnah (clear/visible).
3
Determine the quantity
According to the correct Hanafi view, invalidation occurs even with one clear word. The earlier view of three words is based on a different definition of 'much action' and is not relied upon.
The passage cites al-Muhīt: Muhammad's statement supports one word.
4
Apply the ruling
Test with scenarios: writing one letter? Not a word, so no invalidation. Writing a word on a whiteboard? Invalidates. Writing in the air? Does not invalidate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم كما في الخلاصة وغيرها لو كتب قدر ثلاث كلمات تفسد وإن كان أقل لا فالظاهر تفريعه على أن الكثير ما يستكثره المبتلي به أو أنه ما تكرر ثلاثاً متواليات وأما على الصحيح فالظاهر أن الفساد لا يتوقف على كتابة ثلاث كلمات بل يحصل الفساد بكتابة كلمة واحدة مستبينة على الأرض ونحوها، وقد يشهد بذلك إطلاق ما في المحيط قال محمد: لو كتب في صلاته على شيء فسدت وإن كتب على شيء لا يرى لا تفسد لأنه لا يسمى كتابة .
And as for their statement as in al-Khulāsah and others: If he writes three words, it invalidates, and if less, no. Apparently, it is branched on that much action is what the tested person considers much, or that it is repeated three times consecutively. But according to the correct view, it appears that invalidation is not dependent on writing three words; rather, invalidation occurs with writing one clear word on the ground and the like. This is supported by the absolute statement in al-Muhīt: Muhammad said: If he writes in his prayer on something, it invalidates, but if he writes on something not visible, it does not invalidate because it is not called writing.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage discusses writing during prayer. The statement from al-Khulāsah that invalidates only with three words is explained as being based on the definition of much action. However, the correct view (al-ṣaḥīḥ) presented here is that even one clear word written on a surface invalidates, as indicated by Muhammad's statement in al-Muhīt. Writing on something invisible (e.g., in the air) is not considered writing and thus does not invalidate.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitābah): Writing; forming legible letters on a surface.
مستبينة (mustabīnah): Clear, visible (writing).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view presented in this passage, what writing during prayer invalidates the prayer?
A) Only three or more words on any surface
B) One clear word on a visible surface
C) Any visible mark, even a single dot
D) Writing on the ground only, not on paper

Key Takeaway
One clear word written visibly invalidates the prayer in the Hanafi school.

Moving Feet During Prayer: How Much Invalidates?
Intra-Hanafi critique of a ruling on foot movement in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on moving feet during prayer, distinguishing between little and much movement based on custom, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute's practical consequence.

Core Idea
Moving both feet does not automatically invalidate prayer; only much movement does, as judged by custom.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʼiq critiques a statement in al-Dhakhīrah that distinguishes between moving one foot and both feet. The commentator argues for a more nuanced approach: the quantity of movement, not the number of feet, determines invalidation. Let's analyse the reasoning and the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, note the ruling from al-Dhakhīrah: moving one foot does not invalidate, but moving both feet does. Then, note the commentator's objection and the alternative preferred view that depends on quantity and custom.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, not inter-madhhab.
2
Examine the analogy to hands
The commentator finds the al-Dhakhīrah ruling problematic because moving hands in prayer does not invalidate. By analogy, moving feet should also not invalidate unless the movement is extensive.
The principle of qiyas is used to challenge the distinction.
3
Understand the role of custom
The preferred view leaves the boundary to ʻurf (custom). What is considered 'much' varies by context, making the ruling flexible and practical.
This aligns with the Hanafi reliance on custom in many rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم كما في الذخيرة لو حرك رجلاً لا على الدوام لا تفسد وإن حرك رجليه تفسد فمشكل، لأن الظاهر أن تحريك اليدين في الصلاة لا يبطلها حتى يلحق بهما تحريك الرجلين، فالأوجه قول بعضهم أنه إن حرك رجليه قليلاً لا تفسد وإن كان كثيراً فسدت كما في الذخيرة أيضاً، ولعله مفوض إلى ما يعده العرف قليلاً أو كثيراً .
And as for their statement as in al-Dhakhīrah: If he moves one foot not continuously, it does not invalidate; but if he moves both feet, it invalidates. This is problematic because it is apparent that moving the hands in prayer does not invalidate it, so moving the feet should be analogous. So the more appropriate view is what some say: If he moves his feet a little, it does not invalidate; if much, it invalidates, as also in al-Dhakhīrah. Perhaps it is referred to what custom considers little or much.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on moving feet during prayer. The statement from al-Dhakhīrah distinguishes between moving one foot (no invalidation) and both feet (invalidation). The commentator finds this problematic because moving hands does not invalidate, so feet should follow. The preferred view is that the quantity matters: little movement does not invalidate, much does, with custom determining the boundary. This aligns with the general principle of evaluating actions by their extent.

Key Terms
تحريك الرجلين (taḥrīk al-rijlayn): Moving both feet during prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the passage, what determines whether moving both feet invalidates prayer?
A) The number of feet moved
B) The quantity of movement as judged by custom
C) The intention of the person
D) The duration of movement

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the effect of movement in prayer depends on customary quantity, not merely which limb is moved.

Spousal Kiss and the Man's Prayer: Hanafi Distinction
Effect of husband's kiss on man's prayer with desire distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on a man's prayer being invalidated by a kiss from his wife based on the presence or absence of desire, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi criticism of Ibn al-Humām.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, a man's prayer remains valid if his wife kisses him without his desire; a woman's prayer is invalidated by any spousal contact.

This lesson examines a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: does a man's prayer break if his wife kisses him? The answer hinges on the presence of desire (shahwah) in the man. We will trace the reasoning from al-Khulāsah and the critique leveled by al-Bāqillānī against Ibn al-Humām, clarifying why the genders are treated differently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling in al-Khulāsah
Extract the ruling: a woman's prayer is invalidated by any spousal contact (intercourse, kiss, touch). A man's prayer is NOT invalidated if his wife kisses him without his desire.
This is the relied-upon position (muftā bihī).
2
Contrast with al-Ẓāhiriyyah’s View
Note that some earlier scholars (al-Ẓāhiriyyah) equated both genders, ruling that any spousal contact invalidates the man’s prayer too, based on a presumed analogy.
This view was deemed unnecessary by later commentators.
3
Analyze the Basis for the Distinction
Understand that desire is legally presumed in women (shahwah ghālibah) because of its prevalence, making any contact equivalent to intercourse. In men, desire is not presumed; thus contact without desire is not akin to intercourse.
4
Evaluate the Critique of Ibn al-Humām
Al-Bāqillānī criticizes Ibn al-Humām for overlooking al-Khulāsah’s clear distinction. Recognise that this is a legitimate ikhtilāf within the school, with the sounder view being that of al-Khulāsah.
The tarjīḥ is for al-Khulāsah’s position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : إذا تخمرت المرأة فسدت صلاتها، ولو أغلق الباب لا تفسد، وإن فتح به وهو أن الشهوة غالبة على النساء فهي في حكم الموجودة منها ولهذا حرم نظر الرجل إليها عند غلبة ظنه بالشهوة أو الشك - قالوا - لتحقق الشهوة منها حكماً، وإذا ثبت ذلك كان كثير عمل لوقوعه بين متفاعلين، وإذا قبلته ولم تشته لم يوجد من جانبه أصلاً ويوشح هذا ما مر من اعتبار نزول اللبن كثير عمل ا هـ . لكن ذكر الباقلاني في شرح الملتقي ما لا يحتاج . معه إلى هذا التكلف حيث قال أقول : عبارة الخلاصة لو كانت المرأة في الصلاة فجامعها زوجها تفسد صلاتها وإن لم ينزل مني، وكذا لو قبلها بشهوة أو بغير شهوة أو مسها لأنه في معنى الجماع، وأما لو قبلت المرأة المصلي ولم يشتهها لم تفسد صلاته. هذه عبارة الخلاصة فالعجب من هذا العلامة الإمام ابن الهمام كيف غفل عن الفرق المذكور في هذا المقام ا هـ .
And in al-Zahiriyyah: If a woman covers her head [takhammarat], her prayer is invalidated. And if she closes the door, it does not invalidate. And if she opens it?... And it is that desire is dominant in women, so she is considered as having desire legally; for this reason, a man's looking at her is prohibited when he strongly suspects desire or doubts. They said: because desire is legally established in her. And when that is proven, it is much action due to it occurring between two interacting persons. And if she kissed him and did not desire, no action occurred from her side. This is supported by what passed about considering milk emission as much action. But al-Bāqillānī in his commentary on al-Multaqā mentioned something that does not require this effort, where he said: The wording of al-Khulāsah is: If a woman is in prayer and her husband has intercourse with her, her prayer is invalidated even if he does not emit. Likewise, if he kisses her with desire or without desire, or touches her, because it is like intercourse. But if the woman kisses the praying man and he does not desire her, his prayer is not invalidated. This is the wording of al-Khulāsah. So it is surprising that the eminent scholar Ibn al-Humām overlooked this distinction in this context.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage deals with the effect of spousal actions on a woman's prayer. The initial statement from al-Zahiriyyah is criticized for unnecessary complexity. The correct view, according to al-Bāqillānī quoting al-Khulāsah, is that any intimate contact from the husband (intercourse, kissing, touching) invalidates the woman's prayer, regardless of desire or emission, because it is analogous to intercourse. However, if the woman initiates the kiss without desire, the man's prayer is not invalidated. The commentator sides with al-Bāqillānī and criticizes Ibn al-Humām for missing this clear distinction.

Key Terms
الشهوة (al-shahwah): Sexual desire; legally relevant in determining whether an action invalidates prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī position cited in al-Khulāsah, what is the ruling if a woman kisses a man who is praying, and he does not experience desire?
A) The man's prayer is invalidated because the woman initiated the kiss.
B) The man's prayer remains valid because he did not desire the kiss.
C) The man's prayer is invalid only if the kiss was with desire.
D) Both A and C are correct.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafī school, a man's prayer is not invalidated by his wife's kiss if he feels no desire; this distinguishes his case from a woman's, where any spousal contact invalidates prayer.

Actions That Invalidate Prayer: The Principle of 'Amal Kathīr
Rulings on various actions during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of 'amal kathīr to determine whether a specific action during prayer invalidates it.

Core Idea
A prayer is invalidated by actions considered 'much' by custom, not by mere movement.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq lists dozens of specific actions—opening doors, adjusting garments, handling animals—and rules on each. At first glance it seems like a random list, but a single principle ties them together: the distinction between 'amal qalīl (little action) and 'amal kathīr (much action). Hanafi jurists evaluate actions by custom ('urf): if an action would make an observer doubt that the person is praying, it invalidates. Understanding this principle allows you to derive rulings for new situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing principle
Recognize that all rulings in this passage rest on whether an action is considered 'much' ('amal kathīr) or requires handling (mu'ālaja). Actions that are simple, fast, or customary are tolerated; complex or prolonged ones are not.
Hanafis define 'much' by custom, not by a fixed number. Five different definitions exist, but the relied-upon view is that it is whatever indicates the person is not in prayer.
2
List actions that invalidate
From the text, collect all actions that are said to 'tufsid' (invalidate): opening a door by pushing without handling? (the text says tufsid but this seems contradictory; we will note that later), buttoning a shirt, tightening trousers, putting on a garment, lifting the praying person onto an animal, etc.
Pay attention to the wording: actions that involve handling or a sequence of movements tend to invalidate.
3
List actions that do not invalidate
Similarly, collect actions that are said to 'lā tufsid': removing a shirt, unbuttoning, stepping one or two steps for shade, being lifted without turning from qibla, etc.
These actions are considered little or are done without mu'ālaja.
4
Apply the custom test to a new scenario
Given a new action (e.g., brushing away an insect with one hand), ask: Would a typical onlooker think this person is engaged in prayer or something else? If the action is quick and typical, it likely does not invalidate.
This is the practical tool for a mufti: evaluate by 'urf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو أغلق الباب لا تفسد الخ قال في التجنيس والمزيد : لو فتح باباً أو أغلقه فدفعه بيده من غير معالجة حتى يخرج من الباب المغلق ،تفسد وإن نزع القميص لا تفسد ولو لبس تفسد ولو شد السراويل تفسد ولو لا تفسد ومن أخذ عنان دابته أو مقودها وهو نجس إن كان موضع قبضه نجساً لم يجز، وإن كان النجس موضعاً آخر جاز، وإن كان يتحرك بتحركه هو المختار وإن جذبته الدابة أزالته عن موضع سجوده تفسد. ولو آذاه حر الشمس فتحول إلى الظل خطوة أو خطوتين لا تفسد وقيل في الثلاث كذلك والأول أصح. ولو رفع رجل المصلي عن مكانه ثم وضعه من غير أن يحوله عن القبلة لا تفسد، ولو وضعه على الدابة تفسد. ولو زر قميصاً أو قباء فسدت لا إن حله وإن ألجم دابة فسدت لا إن خلعه ولو لبس خفية فسدت لا إن تنعل أو خلع نعليه كما لو تقلد سيفاً أو نزعه أو وضع الفتيلة في مسرجة أو تروح بمروحة أو بكمه أو سوى من عمامته كوراً أو كورين أو لبس قلنسوة أو بيضة.
His statement: (And if he closes the door, it does not invalidate, etc.) He said in al-Tajnīs and al-Mazīd: If he opens a door or closes it by pushing it with his hand without handling it such that it opens from the closed door, it invalidates. And if he removes a shirt, it does not invalidate; if he puts it on, it invalidates. If he tightens trousers, it invalidates; if he does not... And if he takes the rein of his animal or its halter, and it is najis: if the place he holds is najis, it is not permissible; if the najis is elsewhere, it is permissible. And if it moves with his movement, that is the chosen view. And if the animal pulls him and moves him from his prostration place, it invalidates. And if the sun's heat bothers him and he moves to the shade one or two steps, it does not invalidate; it is said that three steps are also like that, but the first is more correct. And if a man lifts the praying person from his place and then puts him down without turning him from the qibla, it does not invalidate; if he puts him on an animal, it invalidates. And if he buttons a shirt or a cloak, it invalidates, but not if he unbuttons. And if he bridles an animal, it invalidates, but not if he removes the bridle. And if he puts on leggings, it invalidates, but not if he wears sandals or removes them, just as if he girds a sword or removes it, or puts a wick in a lamp, or fans himself with a fan or his sleeve, or straightens his turban two or three folds, or puts on a cap or a helmet.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage lists many specific actions and whether they invalidate prayer. The overall principle is that actions requiring handling (defeating) or that are considered 'much' invalidate, while simple, quick actions do not. For example, opening a door by pushing it open without complex handling does not invalidate if done easily? Actually the text says if he pushes it with his hand without handling (mu'ālaja), it invalidates? Wait, it says 'fadfa'ahu bi yadihi min ghayr mu'ālaja' – that should not invalidate, but the text says 'tufsid'? Let me re-read: 'لو فتح باباً أو أغلقه فدفعه بيده من غير معالجة حتى يخرج من الباب المغلق، تفسد'. This seems to indicate that if he pushes it open without handling, it invalidates? That seems contradictory. The OCR might be unclear. The translation above reflects the Arabic as given. The commentator likely means that such an action is considered much because it requires effort to open a closed door. Similarly, removing a shirt does not invalidate, but putting it on does, as it involves more movement. Tightening trousers invalidates, while loosening does not. Handling a dirty rein is permissible if the held part is clean. Moving one or two steps for shade is allowed. Being lifted by another does not invalidate if orientation is maintained. Buttoning a garment invalidates, but unbuttoning does not. The examples illustrate the distinction between little and much action, with much action being what leads a spectator to doubt that the person is praying.

Key Terms
المعالجة (al-mu'ālaja): Handling; engaging in an action that requires effort or complexity.
العمل الكثير (al-'amal al-kathīr): Much action; an act that by its nature or extent indicates the person is not in prayer.
العمل القليل (al-'amal al-qalīl): Little action; an act that is quick, simple, and customary such that it does not remove the person from the state of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Unbuttoning a shirt – invalidating; buttoning a shirt – not invalidating.
B) Unbuttoning a shirt – not invalidating; buttoning a shirt – invalidating.
C) Both unbuttoning and buttoning are invalidating.
D) Neither unbuttoning nor buttoning invalidates.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school evaluates actions during prayer by custom: 'much' action invalidates, 'little' action does not, and this principle is applied consistently across diverse examples.

Synthesis: Defining 'Much Action' and Harmonizing Rulings
Collecting and reconciling definitions of 'much action' in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five Hanafi definitions of much action in prayer and explain how the first two definitions (observer's doubt and consecutive movements) can harmonize all the derived rulings.

Core Idea
Five definitions of much action exist; the first two suffice to cover all cases.

This lesson synthesizes the various definitions of 'much action' (al-‘amal al-kathīr) in prayer used by Hanafi scholars. The passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq presents five different criteria and shows how the first two—doubt of the observer and consecutive movements—can subsume the rest. Understanding this harmonization is key to applying the rulings correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five definitions
List the five criteria for much action mentioned: (1) observer has no doubt the person is not praying, (2) consecutive movements, (3) action with both hands, (4) action the doer considers much, (5) action intended as a distinct separate activity.
These are from the mashāyikh, not directly from the founding imams.
2
Understand the role of takhrīj
Note that these definitions are derivations (tafrī‘āt) by later scholars, but they are accepted as part of the madhhab because they are derived from established principles (takhrīj).
The author emphasizes this to maintain respect for the scholars.
3
Harmonization by the first two definitions
The author argues that the first two definitions (observer's doubt and consecutive movements) are sufficient to cover all the sub-rulings. For example, using both hands usually leads to observer's doubt, and repetition of three actions creates consecutive movements.
Al-Ḥalabī agrees that most rulings are based on these two.
4
Apply to sample cases
Consider the example of a woman whose husband kisses her with desire during prayer—this invalidates because it clearly indicates non-prayer activity. In contrast, a baby suckling and drawing milk is not considered much action because it is not apparent to the observer.
The fifth definition (using a key) is also classified as little action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the summary: Their furū' in this chapter have differed, and not all are branched on one opinion; rather, some are on one opinion and some on another, as appears to the observer. It is apparent that most of them are derivations of the mashāyikh, not transmitted from the Great Imām. We have mentioned four opinions, and they mentioned a fifth: that much action is what is done with a key of a lock or padlock, but that is disliked and does not invalidate because it is little action. And from Abū Yūsuf: if he closes it, it invalidates, interpreted as if it requires handling. His statement: (And whoever takes the rein of his animal, etc.) has no connection here. His statement: (And the summary that their furū' in this chapter have differed, etc.) I say: It can be said that when the mashāyikh of the madhhab saw the mentioned furū', each of them defined much action by a definition that applies to what he saw of the furū'. By combining the definitions together, all the furū' become organized. So it is said: Much action is what the observer has no doubt that it is not in prayer, or what consists of consecutive movements, or what is done with both hands, or what the tested person considers much, or what is intended by the doer such that he devotes a separate session to it. But the rest of the furū' can be included in the first two, and the other three can be dispensed with. So reflect on what we have mentioned of harmonization, for it includes good opinion of the mashāyikh of the madhhab. Indeed, these furū', even if not all transmitted from the Great Imām, the mashāyikh derived some on transmitted opinions by mere opinion. And what is derived on the madhhab by the people of takhrīj is included in the madhhab. This is what appeared to my deficient intellect, and Allah knows best. Then I saw the scholar Shaykh Ibrāhīm al-Ḥalabī in his commentary on al-Munya mention something similar to what I mentioned, where he said: Most of the furū' or all of them are derived according to one of the two first ways. And it appears that the second is not outside the first, because what is done with both hands usually leads the observer to strongly suspect that he is not in prayer. Likewise, the statement of those who considered repetition up to three consecutive times, because repetition leads to strong suspicion of that. Therefore, the majority of the mashāyikh chose it. His statement: (And they mentioned a fifth opinion, etc.) He said in al-Tatārkhāniyyah from al-Muhīt: This proponent argues by the case of a woman who prayed and her husband touched or kissed her with desire, which invalidates her prayer; likewise, if a baby suckles her breast and milk comes out, it does not invalidate.

Commentary
This concluding passage synthesizes the many rulings on actions that invalidate prayer by providing five definitions of 'much action' (al-‘amal al-kathīr) used by Hanafi mashāyikh: (1) action that leads an observer to doubt the person is in prayer, (2) consecutive movements, (3) action with both hands, (4) action that the person concerned considers much, (5) action intended as a distinct separate activity. The author suggests that the first two definitions suffice to cover all cases, and he harmonizes the different rulings given earlier. He quotes Shaykh Ibrāhīm al-Ḥalabī in support, who says most rulings are based on the first two definitions. The final example from al-Tatārkhāniyyah illustrates that the fifth view (using a key) is little action; it also mentions that a husband's kissing with desire invalidates the woman's prayer, but a baby's suckling with milk does not invalidate because it is not considered much action in that context.

Key Terms
العمل الكثير (al-'amal al-kathīr): Much action; an act that by its nature or quantity indicates the person is not engaged in prayer.
تفريعات المشايخ (tafrī'āt al-mashāyikh): Derivations (sub-rulings) by later Hanafi scholars, not directly from Imam Abu Hanifa.
التخريج (al-takhrīj): Deriving rulings from established principles by analogy or extension.

The Invalidators of Prayer (Mufsidat) in the Hanafi School
Listing and categorizing things that invalidate prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the major invalidators of prayer according to the Hanafi school and distinguish between those that invalidate the essence of the prayer and those that only invalidate its description.

Core Idea
Not every invalidator nullifies the prayer; some only spoil its perfection.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a comprehensive list of actions and events that affect the validity of prayer. The author distinguishes between invalidators that completely nullify the prayer (mufsidat al-asl) and those that affect only its description (mufsidat al-wasf). Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining whether a prayer must be repeated or can be corrected with sajdah al-sahw.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
The passage first lists absolute invalidators (speech, much action, death, etc.), then clarifies that some actions only invalidate the description (wasf), and finally notes that some issues revert to failure of conditions.
2
Extract the absolute invalidators
From the first list: kalam al-nas (human speech), al-amal al-kathir (much action), death, apostasy (by heart), insanity, fainting, every intentional hadath, events requiring ghusl (wet dream, menstruation), woman's alignment under conditions, and leaving a rukn or shart without excuse.
3
Extract the description invalidators
The second list includes: leaving the last tashahhud while intending to return, ability of the one gesturing to perform actual bowing/prostration, remembering a missed prayer, sunrise during Fajr, and Asr time entering during Jumu'ah. These invalidate the wasf, not the asl.
4
Understand sub-cases
Substitution of a reader and prompting another imam are classified under 'much action'. The final set (imam moving ahead, placing women's row in front, praying in a najis place, nudity) revert to failure of a condition, so they are not independent invalidators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها ومن مقصوداً للفاعل بأن أفرد له مجلساً على حدة، ولقد صدق من قال كثرة المقالات تؤذن بكثرة الجهالات، ولقد صدق صاحب الفتاوي الظهيرية حيث قال في الفصل الثالث في قراءة القرآن : إن كل ما لم يرو عن أبي حنيفة فيه قول بقي كذلك مضطرباً إلى يوم القيامة كما حكي عن أبي يوسف أنه كان يضطرب في بعض المسائل وكان يقول : كل مسألة ليس لشيخنا فيها قول فنحن فيها هكذا .اهـ. وإلى هنا تبين أن المفسد للصلاة كلام الناس مطلقاً والعمل الكثير، المفسد الموت والارتداد بالقلب والجنون والإغماء وكل حدث عمد، وما أوجب الغسل كالاحتلام والحيض ومحاذاة المرأة بشروطه وترك ركن من غير قضاء أو شرط لغير عذر. وأما استخلاف القارىء للأمي والفتح على غير إمامه فداخل تحت العمل الكثير، وأما ترك القعدة الأخيرة مع التقييد بالسجدة، وقدرة المومئ على الركوع والسجود، وتذكر صاحب الترتيب الفائتة فيها، وطلوع الشمس في الفجر، ودخول وقت العصر في الجمعة ونظائرها، فما يفسد وصف الفريضة لا أصل الصلاة، وأما فسادها بتقدم الإمام أمام المصلي أو طرحه في التعمد مطلقاً صف النساء أو في مكان نجس أو سقوط الثوب عن عورته مع أداء ركن إن لم يتعمد علم أو لم يعلم ومع المكث قدره إن لم يؤد عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد كما الظهيرية فراجع إلى فوت الشرط كما لا يخفى.
Book of Prayer, Chapter: What Invalidates Prayer and What is Disliked in It. And from [the category of] intended [act] is that one sets aside a separate session. Truly he who said 'multiplying statements indicates multiplying ignorances' spoke truth. And the author of al-Fatawa al-Dhahiriyyah spoke truth when he said in the third chapter on Quranic recitation: 'Every matter not transmitted from Abu Hanifah remained thus unsettled until the Day of Judgment, as is related from Abu Yusuf that he would be unsettled in some issues and would say: every issue in which our shaykh has no statement, we are in it like this.' End. Up to here it is clear that the invalidator of prayer is people's speech absolutely, and much action; the invalidator are death, apostasy by heart, insanity, fainting, and every intentional hadath, and that which requires ghusl like wet dream and menstruation, and a woman's alignment with its conditions, and leaving a pillar without making up or a condition without excuse. As for the substitution of a reader for an unlettered one and prompting someone other than one's imam, it falls under much action. As for leaving the last sitting with the restriction of the prostration, and the ability of the one who gestures to bow and prostrate, and the one who owes prayers remembering a missed one in it, and sunrise in Fajr, and the entry of Asr time in Jumu'ah and the like, these invalidate the description of the obligation, not the essence of the prayer. As for its invalidation by the imam going ahead of the one praying, or his intentionally placing the row of women absolutely, or in a najis place, or the garment falling from his private part while performing a pillar without intentional knowledge or ignorance, and with staying that long without performing [the pillar] according to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad as in al-Dhahiriyyah, it reverts to the failure of a condition as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq serves as a concise listing of the major invalidators (mufsidat) of prayer in the Hanafi school. It begins with a brief preface on the authority of rulings: matters not explicitly narrated from Abu Hanifah remain unsettled. The main content then enumerates: (1) human speech absolutely, (2) much action ('amal kathir), (3) death, apostasy (by heart to avoid physical action), insanity, fainting, (4) any intentional hadath (ritual impurity), (5) events requiring ghusl such as wet dream and menstruation, (6) a woman's alignment with a man in prayer under certain conditions, (7) leaving a rukn without making it up or leaving a shart without excuse. The author then clarifies that some actions only invalidate the 'description' (wasf) of the prayer, not its essence (asl), such as leaving the last sitting while intending to return, or remembering a missed prayer. Finally, actions that occur due to failure of conditions (like impurity of place or nudity) are not independent invalidators but revert to the condition's absence. The passage is a memorization anchor for students.

Key Terms
مفسد (mufsid): Something that invalidates the prayer, rendering it null and requiring repetition.
العمل الكثير (al-'amal al-kathir): Action that is significant in amount and not part of prayer, considered by the Hanafi school to invalidate prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Mortality (death)
C) Sunrise during Fajr prayer
D) Entering of Asr time during Jumu'ah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school categorizes invalidators of prayer into those that nullify the essence (requiring repetition) and those that only spoil the description (correctable by sajdah al-sahw).
